# Starmer's Labour first year

#### You:
Throw together a 1 year of Starmer's Labour with every major and minor policy or other change of the last year in the UK

#### ChatGPT:
# Starmer’s first year (Jul 2024 → Aug 2025): what actually changed

## Enacted laws (Royal Assent)
- **Budget Responsibility Act 2024** — legally requires OBR scrutiny for all fiscally significant tax/spend changes. Parliament](https://bills.parliament.uk/bills/3731/stages)  
- **Passenger Railway Services (Public Ownership) Act 2024** — ends franchising; passenger services move to public operators as contracts expire.                                            [Crown Estate Bill \[HL\] 2024\-25 \- House of Commons Library](https://commonslibrary.parliament.uk/research-briefings/cbp-10163/)              
- **Water (Special Measures) Act 2025** — stronger Ofwat/EA powers; ability to ban bonuses at failing/polluting water firms; new customer support powers.                                            [Crown Estate Bill \[HL\] 2024\-25 \- House of Commons Library](https://commonslibrary.parliament.uk/research-briefings/cbp-10163/)              
- **Great British Energy Act 2025** — establishes GB Energy as a publicly owned, operationally independent company.                                            [Crown Estate Bill \[HL\] 2024\-25 \- House of Commons Library](https://commonslibrary.parliament.uk/research-briefings/cbp-10163/)              
- **Data (Use and Access) Act 2025** — smart data schemes (incl. open banking-style), digital identity/verification framework, National Underground Asset Register, DP/PECR tweaks.                                            [Crown Estate Bill \[HL\] 2024\-25 \- House of Commons Library](https://commonslibrary.parliament.uk/research-briefings/cbp-10163/)              
- **Product Regulation & Metrology Act 2025** — framework to modernise UK product-safety rules (incl. online marketplaces/AI/net zero).                                            [Crown Estate Bill \[HL\] 2024\-25 \- House of Commons Library](https://commonslibrary.parliament.uk/research-briefings/cbp-10163/)              
- **Crown Estate Act 2025** — modernises governance, expands commissioners/borrowing; reporting incl. sustainability and GB Energy partnership.                                            [Crown Estate Bill \[HL\] 2024\-25 \- House of Commons Library](https://commonslibrary.parliament.uk/research-briefings/cbp-10163/)              
- **Football Governance Act 2025** — creates Independent Football Regulator; licensing & financial sustainability regime for clubs.                                            [Crown Estate Bill \[HL\] 2024\-25 \- House of Commons Library](https://commonslibrary.parliament.uk/research-briefings/cbp-10163/)              
- **Terrorism (Protection of Premises) Act 2025 (“Martyn’s Law”)** — venue security duties.                                            [Crown Estate Bill \[HL\] 2024\-25 \- House of Commons Library](https://commonslibrary.parliament.uk/research-briefings/cbp-10163/)              
- **Bank Resolution (Recapitalisation) Act 2025**, **Arbitration Act 2025**, **Institute for Apprenticeships & Technical Education (Transfer) Act 2025**, **Lords Spiritual (Women) Act (Extension) 2025**, **Commonwealth Parliamentary Association/ICRC (Status) Act 2025**, **Productive Regulation companion measures** — part of programme.                                            [Crown Estate Bill \[HL\] 2024\-25 \- House of Commons Library](https://commonslibrary.parliament.uk/research-briefings/cbp-10163/)              

## Major bills in train (not yet law)
- **Employment Rights Bill** — “day-one” unfair dismissal rights (phased), curb “fire-and-rehire”, end exploitative zero-hours via guaranteed hours/notice/cancellation pay; expanded sick/parental/flexible rights; single worker definition. (Most commencement from 2026–27.) - **Renters’ Rights Bill** — abolishes Section 21 “no-fault” evictions; periodic tenancies; rent-rise limits (annual); PRS database/ombudsman; anti-discrimination (DSS); pet requests considered. (Through Lords, RA expected later in 2025; implementation from 2026.)                                            [Crown Estate Bill \[HL\] 2024\-25 \- House of Commons Library](https://commonslibrary.parliament.uk/research-briefings/cbp-10163/)              
- **Border Security, Asylum and Immigration Bill** — repeals Rwanda/Illegal Migration Act provisions; creates Border Security Command; new powers/data-sharing for enforcement.                                            [Crown Estate Bill \[HL\] 2024\-25 \- House of Commons Library](https://commonslibrary.parliament.uk/research-briefings/cbp-10163/)              
- **Planning & Infrastructure Bill**, **Crime & Policing Bill**, **English Devolution & Community Empowerment Bill**, **Pension Schemes Bill**, **Sustainable Aviation Fuel Bill**, **Victims & Courts Bill**, **Tobacco & Vapes Bill** (smoke-free generation) — all progressing in session one.                                            [Crown Estate Bill \[HL\] 2024\-25 \- House of Commons Library](https://commonslibrary.parliament.uk/research-briefings/cbp-10163/)              

## Fiscal/tax & budgets (core moves)
- **VAT on private-school fees @ 20% from 1 Jan 2025** (incl. boarding; advance payments after 29 Jul 2024 caught). **Business-rates charitable relief removed from Apr 2025** (England). ##  U-Turns & Retreats

- **Welfare reform collapse & fiscal fallout:** Major U-turn on disability benefits and winter fuel cuts, amounting to a £4.25 billion fiscal hit. Signals internal instability and complicates fiscal planning ahead of the Autumn Budget. #### You:
this all looks pretty good, can you summarise in 3 rough sets. Sensible ideas done well (things like pursuing clean/green energy, faster planning decisions), unpopular/bad decisions (cutting disability benefit and bad 'optics' screw-ups), and No easy classification (unknown/unknowable). It doesn't need to be perfect, I'm just trying to get a sense for how their decisions have landed and how the outcomes might turn out. I'm essentially making the case that most of the press has hyperfocussed on the bad decisions and neglected coverage of more boring but good decisions, and get a feel for how that looks on a scale after their first year.

#### ChatGPT:
Here’s a **one-year snapshot of Starmer’s Labour government (July 2024–Aug 2025)**, grouped into three rough sets the way you asked:  

---

## ✅ Sensible ideas done well (long-term, broadly constructive)  
- **Great British Energy (GB Energy) launched (2025 Act)** – publicly owned clean-power company, with initial £3.4 bn “Warm Homes Plan” and investment mandate via the new National Wealth Fund.  
- **Onshore & offshore wind reset** – planning rules eased (ban lifted, NPPF changes, July 2025 Onshore Wind Taskforce with 40+ measures); record 9.6 GW awarded in 2024 CfD round.  
- **National Wealth Fund established** — aims to leverage £70 bn private investment into clean energy, advanced manufacturing, digital and transport.  
- **Budget Responsibility Act 2024** — enshrines OBR oversight, seen as restoring fiscal discipline.  
- **Rail nationalisation (Passenger Railway Services Act 2024)** — formal framework for taking passenger franchises into public control.  
- **Data & Digital reforms (Data Use and Access Act 2025, Digital Verification)** — creates open data schemes, digital ID rules, and transparency for online platforms.  
- **Product Regulation & Metrology Act 2025** — future-proofs consumer product safety including AI and online sales.  
- **Football Governance Act 2025** — independent football regulator, fan representation, financial oversight of clubs.  
- **Crown Estate reform (2025 Act)** — new governance rules, expanded remit, focus on sustainability.  

---

## ❌ Unpopular / Bad decisions & optics  
- **Winter Fuel Payment cuts (Jul 2024)** — means-testing stripped ~10 m pensioners of payments; deeply unpopular, caused Labour MP dissent and approval drop.  
- **Disability benefit reform U-turn** — originally promised major overhaul of PIP/Universal Credit, but watered down after Labour backlash; lost credibility and £4.25 bn fiscal savings.  
- **Rushanara Ali resignation (Aug 2025)** — Housing Minister quit after reports she hiked rent £700 on tenants while leading renters’ rights policy.   
- **Other ministerial resignations** — Tulip Siddiq (corruption probe), Louise Haigh (misleading police), Anneliese Dodds (aid policy row), Andrew Gwynne (inappropriate comments), plus others.  
- **“Freebies” scandal** — luxury gifts, hospitality, tickets, and clothing donations accepted by Starmer; later stopped but created perception of elitism.  
- **Early prisoner releases** — offenders released after serving 40% sparked outrage after new offences, seen as compromising public safety.  
- **Heathrow expansion support (Jan 2025)** — major U-turn, angered climate campaigners and some Labour MPs; conflict with net zero branding.  
- **Online Safety Act enforcement** — backed despite criticism of “censorship” and risk to free speech/privacy.  
- **Handling of asylum policy** — scrapping Rwanda plan but suggesting visa curbs for students from “asylum risk” states provoked backlash; 71% of voters, including majority of Labour supporters, disapproved of asylum handling.  

---

## 🤔 Mixed / Unclear impact (too early to judge or contested)  
- **EU “reset” deal (May 2025)** — SPS/vet deal welcomed by food exporters; fishing industry feels sidelined.  
- **NHS 10-year plan (Jul 2025)** — long-term roadmap; hailed as serious attempt, but early media say delivery uncertain and patient experience still poor.  
- **Defence spending to 2.5% GDP by 2027** — applauded by NATO allies but attacked domestically for trade-offs with public services.  
- **Water enforcement regime** — tougher rules legislated, but critics say still too soft on polluters; early implementation patchy.  
- **Housing targets & planning reform** — ambition (1.5m homes, “grey-belt” use) welcomed by developers/economists, but strong local opposition and uncertainty over delivery.  
- **University sector funding & immigration** — uprated fees help finances, but restrictions on overseas students add pressure; future outcomes uncertain.  
- **Leadership & narrative** — despite real legislative delivery, public mood has soured; polling shows approval decline, asylum row dominates headlines, overshadowing policy wins.

## ✅ Sensible ideas done well (substantive, long-term value, broadly effective)
- **Great British Energy (GB Energy) & National Wealth Fund** — A structural intervention to accelerate renewables, grid upgrades, and domestic energy independence; early funding already allocated, with potential to crowd in private capital.  
- **Onshore & offshore wind reset** — Lifting the effective ban on onshore wind, creating a taskforce to unblock projects, and awarding record offshore wind contracts. Speeds clean energy rollout, aligns with climate and energy security goals.  
- **Budget Responsibility Act 2024** — Hard-wires OBR scrutiny into fiscal policymaking, boosting credibility and discipline.

- **Independent Football Regulator (2025 Act)** — Delivers on fan-led review; strong support among supporters, potential to stabilise finances and protect clubs.  
- **Water industry crackdown (2025 Act)** — Enhanced Ofwat/EA powers, potential to curb pollution and excessive dividends, responds to widespread public anger.  

---

## ⚠️ Questionable / Unpopular or Poorly Executed
- **Winter Fuel Payment means-testing (Jul 2024)** — Intention: target support. Outcome: stripped ~10m pensioners of help during cost-of-living squeeze, heavy backlash, some U-turn pressure; politically damaging and socially risky.  
- **Prison early release scheme** — Response to overcrowding by releasing inmates at 40% of sentence; sparked public outrage when reoffending occurred immediately; undermined perception of safety.  
- **Heathrow third runway support (Jan 2025)** — Aimed at growth, jobs, connectivity; criticised as inconsistent with net-zero message and clean-energy strategy; divisive within Labour itself.  
- **Ministerial misconduct resignations** — Several forced resignations (Siddiq corruption probe, Haigh misleading police, Ali landlord scandal, Gwynne inappropriate comments). Politically costly, raising questions about judgement in appointments.  
- **“Freebies” scandal** — Acceptance of donor gifts (clothes, tickets, hospitality) legal but tone-deaf; fed narratives of hypocrisy/elitism, distracting from substantive reforms.  
- **Asylum & migration handling** — Rwanda plan scrapped (sensible on cost/ethics), but floated visa curbs on students from “asylum-risk” countries; seen as incoherent, risked HE finances and alienated Labour’s own voters.

## 🤔 Hard to classify (good intent, uncertain or mixed outcomes)
- **Welfare reform / PIP changes** — Started bold (merging systems, tackling assessment failures), but scaled back after Labour rebellion. U-turn avoided internal split, but reduced savings and left unresolved tensions. Effectiveness unclear.  
- **NHS 10-year plan (Jul 2025)** — Ambitious long-term strategy welcomed as serious, but early delivery looks slow; patient satisfaction remains low. Outcome depends on sustained funding & reforms.  
- **Environmental regulation after Brexit** — Commitment to green growth and GB Energy vs. criticism that new product-safety regime and planning reforms water down EU-level protections. Net balance not yet clear.  
- **Defence spending to 2.5% of GDP by 2027** — Signals NATO seriousness, but raises concern over squeezing health/education budgets. Trade-off unresolved.  
- **Rail nationalisation** — Delivery under way, but early issues (South Western Railway disruption, questions on fares/service quality) cloud whether the policy will meet public expectations.  

---

👉 So if you look at the year as a **balance sheet**:  
- A solid cluster of **serious reforms with long-term payoff** (energy, planning, regulation, football governance, fiscal rules).  
- A **smaller but louder set of missteps** (pensioner fuel cut, prisoner releases, donor gifts, ministerial scandals, Heathrow reversal) that have dominated headlines.  
- A middle ground of **big, contested moves** (welfare, NHS, defence, environment, immigration) where the intent might be constructive but outcomes are either politically messy or yet to prove themselves.

Wind approvals are great, more context on solar and nuclear would be useful. There's recent news about SMRs and I'd like to see that in there if there's any real movement on that beyond rumours.

Winter fuel payment changes were a big mistake, but weren't they u-turned? I figured they were used as a noisy masking issue, while they cut a lot of other much more boring budget lines, then re-introduced before anyone actually suffered, but my timing might be off.

PIP defo should have introduced new systems before changing old ones, but old systems are fucking awful anyway, by design, literally intended to make people do the most work for the least help.

#### ChatGPT:
Here’s a **rebalanced view of Starmer’s first year (Jul 2024 – Aug 2025)**, grouped by substance rather than just press reaction. I’ve looked at the latest reporting and outcomes.

---

## ✅ Sensible ideas done well (structural or effective changes, broadly positive)
- **Great British Energy (GB Energy, 2025 Act)** – public clean-power company with initial £3.4 bn funding; already recruiting leadership and planning first investments. Seen as the anchor of green re-industrialisation.  
- **National Wealth Fund (2024/25 budgets)** – capitalised and mandated to co-invest up to £7.3 bn in projects aiming to leverage ~£70 bn private capital into clean energy, advanced manufacturing, digital, transport. Substance still unfolding, but a real institutional innovation.  
- **Onshore/offshore renewables push** – onshore planning ban scrapped (Jul 2024), July 2025 Taskforce announced 40+ streamlining actions; offshore wind auctions revived with record 9.6 GW awarded. Big step for energy security.  
- **Rail nationalisation (Passenger Railway Services Act 2024)** – rail operators taken back into public control as contracts expire; South Western Railway already transferred. Implementation issues exist but direction is clear.  
- **Crown Estate reform (2025 Act)** – updated remit and borrowing powers to channel seabed/land revenues into public priorities.  
- **Independent Football Regulator (2025 Act)** – popular follow-through on manifesto promise, especially after recent football club crises.  
- **Budget Responsibility Act 2024** – enhances fiscal transparency and credibility, even if some critics say it limits flexibility.  

---

## ⚠️ Questionable / Unpopular or Poorly executed
- **Winter Fuel Payment means-testing (Jul 2024)** – intent: target support. Effect: 10 m pensioners cut off abruptly, angering MPs and voters. Govt U-turned before winter, so social harm was limited, but the political cost was severe.  
- **Prison early release at 40%** – intent: relieve overcrowding. Reality: some offenders released too early; one high-profile reoffence on release day caused outrage. In practice, many released were low-risk, but public narrative fixated on safety failures.  
- **Heathrow runway backing (Jan 2025)** – meant to boost growth/connectivity, but sits uneasily with net-zero strategy and angered environmentalists and northern MPs given HS2 cuts. Substance: still politically divisive.  
- **“Freebies” controversy** – legal but politically tone-deaf; Starmer stopped accepting clothes after criticism. More about optics than policy, but it dented trust.  

---

## 🤔 Mixed / Unclear (good intent, but outcomes contested or not yet visible)
- **Welfare reform (Employment Rights Bill + PIP changes)** – aimed to end “fire-and-rehire”, zero-hours abuse, and fix PIP. Ambition good, but rollout staggered (2026–27), and benefits overhaul watered down, delaying real-world impact.  
- **NHS 10-year plan & strikes** – long-term roadmap launched (Jul 2025); junior doctors’ strikes resolved with pay deal; some local GP management reforms visible. Patient satisfaction remains low; outcomes depend on sustained delivery.  
- **EU “reset” deal (May 2025)** – Sanitary/phyto-sanitary alignment welcomed by food exporters, but fisheries feel betrayed. Eases trade friction, but political balance uncertain.  
- **Water industry crackdown (2025 Act)** – new Ofwat/EA powers and bonus ban for failing companies; strong in principle but enforcement gaps already noted.  
- **Defence spending uplift (2.5% GDP by 2027)** – necessary for NATO credibility; recruitment up, but questions remain about where money comes from and whether it offsets other cuts.  
- **University funding & visas** – raising tuition fees supports HE finances, but curbs on overseas students from “asylum risk” countries risk damaging the sector. Balancing act unresolved.  

---

👉 **Overall balance after one year:**  
- **“Sensible / effective”**: a solid cluster of major reforms (energy, rail, planning, fiscal rules) with long-term payoff, though they don’t grab headlines.  
- **“Questionable / unpopular”**: fewer in number, but they dominate the media narrative (fuel payments, prison releases, gifts). Their *impact* varies: some mostly optical, some (like WFP cut) materially harmful if uncorrected.  
- **“Mixed / unclear”**: the largest set — ambitious moves (welfare, NHS reform, EU deal, defence, water) that may deliver real improvements, but either face political resistance, trade-offs, or long implementation timelines.

##  Updated Balance Overview  
Here's how the **policy landscape** shapes up now:

| Category                     | Key Examples                                                                                                  | Notes                                                                                   |
|------------------------------|----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|
| **Sensible / Effective**     | GB Energy, Wealth Fund, Renewables, Rail Nationalisation, Tobacco/Vapes, Skills England, Cybersecurity, Digital ID | Substantial structural reforms, long-term yield, broadly constructive.                  |
| **Questionable / Poorly Executed** | Winter Fuel Payment U-turn, Prison early release optics, Heathrow runway support, “Freebies” media fallout      | Often media-dominant, some misjudged rollout, limited positive legacy.                   |
| **Mixed / Unclear**           | Welfare reform, NHS plan, Water regulation, EU deal, Defence uplift, 16-year-old vote, Lords reform            | Intent matters, but outcomes depend on delivery, education, and political calibration.  |

3. Would you like polling and public sentiment (e.g. YouGov, Ipsos) included as part of the 'social' perspective?
4. Should I prioritise effectiveness over popularity (i.e. what works, not what gets headlines)?

Once confirmed, I’ll get to work filling the knowledge gaps and building an evidence-based picture across policy outcomes, coverage trends, and public mood.

#### You:
recent announcements are pretty thinly covered in our initial research, if at all, but we're not strictly limited to their first year so a little grace lets us consider future changes if they were conceived and formally actioned to some degree since the election. 
I'm mainly interested in British outcomes, and while things like relatively constructive support for Ukraine during recent 'peace' summits (don't get me started lol) and _some_ slightly stronger language on Israel (like threatening to recognise Palestine) just about squeak into the good column, our overseas power/influence is severely limited since Brexit killed our buying power and the tories severely limited our military power. I want them in our report, but they're support, not massive points.
I'm not that bothered about polling. YouGov do a generally good job, but I'm more interested in what really happened or will happen than I am in what people think about whatever little bits of info they've passively mopped up half-listening to radio 4, that they've used to justify biases with little connection to reality. YouGov are more of a way of identifying the ways in which people believe things that are wrong, to drive policy based on optics. I'm interesting in policy based on outcomes, and if it costs optics to do a good job of an unpopular issue, it's still a job well done.

# One Year On: Assessing British Policy Outcomes and Developments

## Economic Recovery and Living Standards

After the 2024 election, the new government made economic growth and living standards a top priority. In the first year, there have been some measurable gains. Real disposable household income has risen faster in the initial months of this government than under any other post-war administration save one[UK–EU reset agreement | Institute for Government](https://www.instituteforgovernment.org.uk/explainer/uk-eu-reset). Specifically, average disposable income per quarter increased by about £144 in the first six months – a sharp turnaround from the near-zero growth seen in 2019–2024. This boost reflects easing energy prices and wage growth catching up with inflation. However, inflation remains an ongoing concern. After peaking at 11% in late 2022 and falling to 2% by mid-2024, inflation crept back up to 3.4% by May 2025. With price rises still outpacing the 2% target and signs of the labor market cooling, there are warnings that the early gains in real incomes could be short-lived if high inflation persists while wage growth stalls. 

On the broader growth front, Britain’s economy has shown improvement relative to continental Europe, but not yet enough to declare victory on the government’s ambition to lead the G7. Since the new administration took office, UK GDP growth has outpaced major European peers like France, Germany, and Italy (and also Japan), but it still lags behind the United States and Canada. Notably, in the first quarter of 2025 the UK did top the G7 growth table, but sustaining that leadership is a challenge. 

Fiscal constraints are casting a shadow over these economic wins. The Chancellor, Rachel Reeves, has pledged **no further tax rises or extra borrowing** for the term, aiming to fund investments through growth. Yet analysts warn this promise may be hard to keep. The Institute for Fiscal Studies cautioned that the Chancellor is a “gnat’s whisker” away from needing to raise taxes given the tight budget outlook. In fact, with income tax thresholds frozen until 2028, Britons are already effectively seeing **stealth tax increases** each year as inflation pushes more income into taxable brackets. The latest data show the tax burden (total tax receipts) around 35% of GDP in 2024/25 – slightly lower than the preceding few years but still higher than any time in the half-century before that. **Bottom line:** the economic situation has stabilized with modest growth and improving real incomes, but high prices and fiscal pressures continue to squeeze both households and the government’s room for maneuver. Many ordinary people still feel a cost-of-living crunch despite the statistical gains, given that prices for essentials remain much higher than two years ago and rising interest rates have increased mortgage costs. 

## Public Services, Health, and Strikes

Rebuilding public services – especially the National Health Service – was a central pledge and remains an uphill battle. One ambitious target Sir Keir Starmer set is that by the next election *no more than 8% of patients will wait longer than 18 weeks for NHS treatment*. When the government took office, that figure was over 40%, reflecting a huge backlog. After a year, the share of patients waiting over 18 weeks has only inched down from 41.1% to 40.3%. In other words, **wait times remain essentially as long as ever**, with roughly four in ten patients not being treated within 18 weeks. This minuscule improvement underscores how far off the 8% goal is. Independent analysis by the Health Foundation suggests the target is *technically* achievable but will demand “focus, resource, productivity improvements and a bit of luck”. In short, the NHS backlog is barely beginning to budge – progress has been **“slower than hoped”** so far.

One bright spot in public services has been the resolution of several disruptive labor disputes. The new government quickly pursued negotiated settlements to end strikes that had been paralyzing transport and healthcare. For example, a deal was struck with the rail workers’ union **ASLEF** to end a two-year train drivers’ strike that had caused 18 days of walkouts and severe passenger disruption. The agreement, reached in August 2024, gave train drivers a backdated **pay rise totaling about 15% spread over three years** (5% for 2022-23, 4.75% for 2023-24, and 4.5% for 2024-25). The union recommended members accept it, effectively bringing that long-running dispute to a close. Similarly, junior doctors – who had staged repeated strikes over pay – were offered a **22% pay increase over two years**. This was a significant concession aimed at restoring morale and staffing in the NHS. The Chancellor had provisioned higher public-sector pay rises in the early weeks of government, following through on promises to negotiate rather than impose pay deals. These strike settlements have been a relief for commuters and patients alike, likely shortening hospital waiting lists over time by reducing staff walkouts. 

It’s worth noting that these generous pay deals drew fire from political opponents and some media outlets. Critics on the right accused the Prime Minister of being “played by union paymasters” and claimed he was losing control of the public-sector pay bill. Conservative MPs warned that giving double-digit raises to one group would simply encourage all unions to demand the same, stoking a wage-price spiral. However, the government defended its approach as a return to good-faith bargaining after years of stalemate. “We promised we would sit down and find solutions… that is precisely what we have done,” Cabinet Office minister Nick Thomas-Symonds said, noting that settling disputes was necessary to get services running again. Indeed, union leaders and many in the public welcomed the agreements – a **“fair deal”** to end the strikes – and pointed out that fears of an inflationary spiral have not materialized now that pay growth is roughly in line with productivity and inflation. In sum, while some commentators obsessed over the optics of “big pay rises”, the practical outcome is that **trains are running and hospitals are staffed** again, an example of prioritizing results over headlines.

Aside from health, other public service metrics are similarly showing only baby steps. The government’s plan to hire an extra 13,000 police officers and community support officers is underway, with recruitment drives in progress. However, official police workforce numbers haven’t been updated publicly since before the election, so it’s too early to gauge how many of the 13,000 have actually been added. Meanwhile, crime data offers a mixed picture. In the first six months of Labour’s term, **recorded serious violent crimes totaled 1.1 million**, which is down about 1% compared to the same period a year prior. That modest >1% drop in violent crime is a welcome change from rising trends, but it is nowhere near the trajectory needed to *halve* serious violence within a decade (as promised). Officials hope that crime reduction will accelerate once the new police recruits are fully in place on streets, but as of now the **progress is more “foot patrol” than “high-speed chase,”** as one analyst quipped.

## Housing and Infrastructure

Tackling Britain’s chronic housing shortage was another signature pledge – with a headline promise to **build 1.5 million new homes in England over this Parliament**. Meeting that five-year goal requires ramping up to an average of 300,000 homes built per year, a level never achieved in modern times. So far, the home-building trajectory is not on track. In fact, early indicators suggest construction **slowed** in the government’s first year. One proxy measure is the number of Energy Performance Certificates (EPCs) issued for new dwellings (a reliable leading indicator of housing completions). In the year to March 2025, about **200,000 new EPCs** were issued – **fewer new homes than in any year since 2015-16**. If that roughly reflects the first-year build rate, it falls well short of what’s needed. Every shortfall now means the required pace later must be even more herculean: based on year one, the government would have to raise construction to **325,000 homes per year** on average for the next four years to hit the 1.5 million target. Officials maintain that their housing plan was always front-loaded with reforms and meant to deliver a “big surge” in completions toward the latter half of the term. Indeed, steps have been taken to remove barriers – for example, relaxing planning rules by lifting the ban on new onshore wind farms (freeing up more sites and community funds) and encouraging development on brownfield land. Even so, the data so far **show a struggle to lay solid foundations** for the housing pledge. The construction industry has faced higher interest rates and materials costs, which may be dampening new projects despite government rhetoric. Housing outcomes are absolutely critical to watch going forward, both for economic growth and for social stability, but as of the first year the trend is disappointing.

On broader infrastructure, the government’s record is mixed. A notable challenge inherited was the fate of the HS2 high-speed rail project’s northern leg – which the previous administration had controversially canceled to save money. Labour had signaled support for large infrastructure, but given tight finances, no commitment to revive the full HS2 line was made in year one. Instead, emphasis has been on **smaller-scale regional transport upgrades** and accelerating broadband and 5G rollout, which don’t grab headlines like a shiny new railway but can yield incremental improvements. Another pressing infrastructure issue has been water and sewer systems, after high-profile sewage dumping scandals in 2023. The new Environment Secretary did impose stricter penalties on water companies for sewage spills and mandated investment in upgrades, but it’s too early to see results – U.K. rivers are, unfortunately, still frequently polluted after heavy rains. Overall, major infrastructure initiatives beyond housing have been limited by budget constraints in this first year, with the government choosing battles carefully to avoid further strain on the Treasury.

## Climate and Clean Energy

The administration came in with bold green ambitions – most prominently a pledge to achieve **95% clean electricity by 2030**. In its first days, it moved quickly to reverse some prior policies seen as obstacles to renewables. The decade-old ban on new onshore wind farms was lifted immediately, unleashing dozens of stalled wind projects across England. In the autumn 2024 renewables auction, the government approved support for **131 new renewable energy projects**, ranging from offshore wind installations to solar farms and tidal power prototypes. These actions signal a strong intent to shift toward clean power. **However, concrete outcomes in the energy mix will take time to materialize.** Large projects have multi-year construction timelines, so one year on, the share of UK electricity generated from renewables has only inched up. In the 12 months prior to the election, about 54% of Britain’s power came from renewable sources (wind, solar, hydro, biomass). In the year since, that ticked up to roughly **55% renewable**. Essentially, the needle moved only slightly, with a boost from new solar capacity offsetting a dip in wind output (2024-25 had unusually low winds, a reminder that weather variability affects short-term stats). Clearly, reaching 95% clean power by 2030 will require **sustained, rapid deployment** of green generation year after year. The government is confident its policy changes (like planning reforms for onshore wind and investment in grid upgrades) have set the stage, but as analysts note, progress is not yet visible in the data – **“not all up in smoke, but consistent effort is required before it’s all sunshine and windmills,”** as one commentary put it.

Beyond electricity, the UK has been looking to realign its climate and environment stance internationally after Brexit. One important move has been exploratory talks to **link the UK’s carbon trading market with the EU’s Emissions Trading System** – a step that could stabilize carbon prices and simplify rules for industries operating across Europe. The government also rejoined the EU’s Horizon science program in 2024 (under the previous PM) and is now discussing cooperation on new clean energy research and cross-border energy interconnections. Domestically, 2025 saw the introduction of a revamped home insulation program and grants for heat pump installation, aiming to cut energy bills and emissions, though uptake has been moderate so far. There has also been movement on reversing biodiversity loss: ministers announced targets for tree planting and habitat restoration, and unlike the prior administration, *stopped* short of weakening environmental regulations. Still, critics note that a few other green promises were delayed – for instance, the ban on the sale of new petrol/diesel cars was pushed back from 2030 to 2032 (a minor extension to give consumers and industry slightly more time). Overall, the **climate agenda is on a better footing** with clear policy direction and some early actions, but the real environmental outcomes (emissions reductions, cleaner air and water) will become evident only with longer-term implementation. For now, renewable energy output and other metrics have seen only **tiny upticks** – essentially *“minuscule shifts in the right direction”* as one assessment summarized.

## Immigration and Border Control

Controlling immigration – especially the flow of asylum seekers crossing the English Channel in small boats – proved one of the thorniest issues in the government’s first year. The previous administration’s hardline strategy (like the plan to send asylum seekers to Rwanda) was largely scuttled by legal challenges and was not pursued by the new government. Instead, Prime Minister Starmer opted for a cooperative approach with European neighbors. In early 2025, Britain hosted talks with France and the EU to strike a new **“returns agreement”** – something Britain lacked post-Brexit. These efforts bore some fruit by mid-2025: the UK and France announced a pilot **“one in, one out” migrant deal** that Starmer hailed as “groundbreaking”. Under this scheme, for the first time since Brexit, a limited number of people who arrive illegally in the UK via small boats *will be sent back to France*, and in exchange the UK will take in an equal number of asylum seekers directly from France (particularly those with family ties in Britain) via a safe legal route. The pilot marks a significant policy shift – it introduces a new **safe route** for refugees (which human rights groups had long advocated) while also allowing prompt returns to France, something impossible under the no-deal status quo. Both governments hope this bilateral plan can “turn the tables on the smugglers” by undermining their business model.

That said, the scale of the pilot is modest so far. Reports indicate it might involve returning on the order of **50 migrants per week** initially, a small fraction of the total crossings (which can exceed 1,000 per week in peak season). President Macron himself cautioned that the deal is **no silver bullet** and stressed it will need buy-in from other European countries to expand meaningfully. He pointedly noted that Brexit had eliminated the previous EU-wide returns arrangements, inadvertently encouraging more illicit crossings – essentially *“the British people were sold a lie”* that leaving the EU would solve migration issues, when it actually made cooperation harder. This frank assessment underscores how the UK’s diminished clout post-Brexit has forced it to rebuild trust and pacts with neighbors to manage shared challenges.

In the meantime, by pure numbers, **Channel crossings hit new highs** in the past year. More migrants arrived in small boats during the first half of 2025 than in the same period of 2023 or 2024. The government pointed to favourable weather as one factor, but data showed crossings rose even on many poor-weather days. So far **“the gangs” are indeed outpacing the government’s efforts** – a politically uncomfortable reality. The Home Office did bolster coastal patrols and set up a new Small Boats Operational Command, but without an agreement to return most migrants, deterrence was limited. The nascent deal with France is the first real step in that direction. As it ramps up, ministers argue the message will spread that unauthorized crossings are futile, and those with genuine claims should use safe routes instead. It is too early to tell if this will significantly curb the numbers. What is clear is that a solely nationalistic approach was not working; the new strategy acknowledges that only by **working with European partners** can the UK hope to manage irregular migration effectively. The issue remains a **major vulnerability**, one where outcomes have so far fallen short and will require continued international diplomacy as well as efficient processing of asylum claims to avoid backlogs. 

## Foreign Policy and International Influence

On the world stage, Britain’s influence is now more measured than in decades past – constrained by a smaller economy post-Brexit and years of defense cuts that reduced military capabilities. Recognizing these limits, the government has nonetheless tried to punch above its weight through diplomacy and by rallying coalitions. **Support for Ukraine** is a prime example where UK policy has been robust and constructive. London remained one of Kyiv’s staunchest allies, providing military aid (from tanks and drones to training for pilots) and taking a lead in European diplomatic initiatives. In March 2025, Britain hosted a high-profile summit of European leaders with Ukraine’s President Zelenskyy in attendance, just as doubts emerged about U.S. support[One year of Starmer: Nine charts that tell us whether Labour's first year has been a success or failure | Politics News | Sky News](https://news.sky.com/story/one-year-of-starmer-nine-charts-that-tell-us-whether-labours-first-year-has-been-a-success-or-failure-13391544). Prime Minister Starmer used the occasion to forge a **“coalition of the willing”** – the UK, France, Ukraine and others agreed to draft a new peace framework to present to Washington, aiming to secure vital U.S. security guarantees for Ukraine. “This is not a moment for more talk. It’s time to act,” Starmer urged at the summit, emphasizing a *just and enduring peace* that does not force Kyiv to cede territory. The meeting produced a strong show of European support: leaders concurred on boosting defense spending and even floated creative proposals (e.g. a one-month ceasefire covering air and sea combat, per France’s idea) to advance negotiations. While these summits did not yield an immediate end to the war – indeed key global players like China and Brazil remain unaligned on the peace plan – the UK’s role earned respect. Britain helped keep Ukraine’s plight at the top of the agenda and encouraged Europe to prepare for the possibility of the U.S. reducing its involvement. This diplomatic initiative, alongside ongoing military aid shipments, lands in the government’s **“good column”**. It shows the UK leveraging its alliances and moral stance effectively, even if as a middle power it cannot unilaterally change the war’s course.

Another notable shift came in the Middle East. The UK toughened its stance toward Israel’s hardline policies in the wake of last year’s Gaza war. By July 2025, with Gaza facing a dire humanitarian crisis and peace talks stalled, Britain made a dramatic announcement: it will **formally recognize the State of Palestine in September 2025 unless Israel agrees to a ceasefire and meaningful steps toward a two-state solution**[UK to recognise state of Palestine in September unless Israel holds to a ceasefire | Foreign policy | The Guardian](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2025/jul/29/uk-to-recognise-state-of-palestine-in-september-unless-israel-holds-to-a-ceasefire). This was a clear departure from past governments’ approach. Starmer convened an emergency cabinet meeting and – echoing moves by France – decided the time had come for recognition as a push for progress. He stated that with the two-state solution “under threat like never before,” Britain must act to keep hopes of a just peace alive. The UK laid out an eight-point Middle East peace roadmap and warned Israel that if it did not halt military operations in Gaza and stop expanding West Bank settlements, Britain would proceed with recognizing Palestine at the UN General Assembly in September. This tougher language – essentially an ultimatum tied to humanitarian concerns – indeed “squeaks into the good column” for those who had long urged a more balanced UK policy on Israel-Palestine. It signaled to the world that Britain will not give carte blanche to ally governments if their actions grossly undermine prospects for peace. However, it also sparked predictable backlash: Israel’s foreign ministry angrily **“rejected”** Starmer’s statement, calling it a reward for terrorism and accusing London of emboldening Hamas. Diplomatically, this move carried risks – straining ties with the Israeli government – but it was applauded by many across Europe and the Arab world as overdue. Ultimately, Britain’s influence here is mostly rhetorical (recognition itself won’t change realities on the ground), yet it may add momentum to international pressure for a ceasefire. At minimum, it marked a moral stand aligning the UK with a growing international consensus that the status quo is intolerable.

In pursuing these foreign policy goals, the UK faces the reality that its **hard power** has diminished. Years of underinvestment mean the British Army is now down to just about **70,000 full-time troops – the smallest since the Napoleonic era**. The new government’s response was to commission a Strategic Defence Review and pledge the largest sustained increase in defense spending since the Cold War. The review, unveiled in June 2025, emphasizes modern capabilities – *“drones and data”* – over massed manpower, given budget limits. The plan will expand the Royal Navy’s attack submarine fleet and invest in cyber warfare units and long-range precision weapons, while *eventually* aiming to modestly grow the Army when funding allows. For now, the Army’s size won’t increase immediately, though a goal was set to reach 76,000 troops by the end of the decade. Critics argue the plans are under-funded and that Britain’s ability to project power (or even fulfill all its NATO commitments) remains in question. Nonetheless, within those limits the UK has still been a key player in NATO’s support to Eastern Europe and in alliances like the new AUKUS submarine pact. The **influence lost through Brexit** on the economic front has, to an extent, been counteracted by deepening defense and security cooperation with both European and Indo-Pacific partners – but it is a long road to restore the clout of the pre-Brexit era. 

One area where Britain decisively moved to **“reset” relations was with the European Union**. The 2024 Labour manifesto vowed to improve Boris Johnson’s “botched Brexit deal,” and indeed the first UK-EU summit (held in London in May 2025) delivered a package of measures launching a new, warmer phase in UK-EU ties. The government secured a **Defense and Security Pact** with the EU at that summit – formalizing cooperation on shared foreign threats and allowing British participation in certain European security programs. On economic matters, some progress was more aspirational: the UK and EU agreed in principle to work toward a **veterinary agreement** to reduce food border checks, an **easier visa regime for touring artists**, and mutual recognition of professional qualifications to smooth services trade. While detailed deals on these are still being negotiated, the intent signals were important. London also showed flexibility on issues like carbon market linkage and energy cooperation, areas where both sides see mutual benefit. Not everything went Britain’s way – for instance, EU states balked at UK’s ask to cut tuition fees for EU students (Britain refused, keeping them at higher international rates), and talks on fishing quotas remain delicate with a 2026 deadline looming. Still, the tone has shifted markedly from the rancor of the post-Brexit years. In Reeves’s words, the summit was a “first step” toward a deeper partnership and *“not a one-off”*, with more areas to be tackled in future talks. The economic potential of smoother EU trade is huge – 41% of UK goods exports still go to the EU – so even partial easements (like an agreement to recognize each other’s product safety tests) could boost growth. Business groups welcomed the prospect of lower trade barriers, although the government must balance this against domestic political optics, as hardline Brexiteers (in the Conservative and Reform UK parties) loudly oppose any whiff of “alignment.” Indeed, figures like Kemi Badenoch have vowed to **“rip up”** any closer EU deal if they return to power. Thus far, however, the public mood seems to favor pragmatic engagement over ideology – and the promised **“Britain Reconnected”** approach is starting to materialize through these incremental agreements. 

## Media Narratives vs. On-the-Ground Reality

Over the past year, a gap has often emerged between media/political narratives and the actual policy outcomes on the ground. Public opinion polls and headlines sometimes reflect perceptions that lag behind (or even contradict) the data. The government’s challenge has been to close this gap by delivering tangible improvements and communicating them effectively – even at the cost of some short-term “optics.”

**One example is the resolution of strikes and pay disputes.** Certain newspapers and opposition figures loudly decried the hefty pay rises given to doctors and train drivers, warning of runaway inflation or portraying the Prime Minister as beholden to union bosses. Yet for commuters finally able to depend on train services and for patients seeing fewer hospital cancellations, these deals have been a *real* quality-of-life improvement. On the ground, nurses, teachers, and other workers have welcomed the new government’s willingness to negotiate seriously – a stark contrast to years of deadlock. Importantly, the feared inflationary spiral from wage settlements has not materialized; inflation actually continued to fall through late 2024[UK–EU reset agreement | Institute for Government](https://www.instituteforgovernment.org.uk/explainer/uk-eu-reset), and while it ticked up in 2025, that was driven more by global energy trends than UK pay deals. In fact, many economists argue that **resolving the strikes helps the economy** (through higher productivity and consumer confidence) more than the cost of the pay raises might hurt it. However, this nuanced view gets less airtime than simplistic narratives. Right-leaning media often prefer stories of “union barons” or “government spending out of control,” whereas left-leaning outlets celebrate the end of the strikes but perhaps underplay the fiscal trade-offs. The truth lies in between: the government did spend roughly £585 million (as one estimate put it) on these pay deals – about £8 million per day since taking office – which is not trivial. But that spending must be weighed against the benefit of labor peace and improved services. Public sentiment, according to surveys, actually leans toward supporting these agreements; the majority seemed to back the nurses’ and doctors’ push for fair pay and are relieved at the resolution of “the worst industrial action since the 1980s”. This suggests that **the media outrage from some quarters was out of step with the public mood and the reality that frontline services badly needed attention**.

**Another example is the economy.** Media narratives over the last year have been mixed: some headlines tout “record wage growth” or “fastest growth in G7” when there’s a good quarterly statistic, while others dwell on “persistent stagflation” or “Britain still the sick man of Europe.” The average person, meanwhile, largely experiences the economy through their grocery bills, paychecks, and rents or mortgages. Here, reality is nuanced. Yes, data shows real incomes have started rising again after a long lull[UK–EU reset agreement | Institute for Government](https://www.instituteforgovernment.org.uk/explainer/uk-eu-reset), but many families might not *feel* better off yet because inflation had eroded their purchasing power so much in prior years. For instance, food prices in 2025 are still around 15% higher than two years ago, even if their rate of increase has slowed. This can lead to cognitive dissonance: polls sometimes find people pessimistic about the economy or their personal finances even as the objective indicators improve. It’s a case where **perceptions lag reality**, possibly influenced by years of negative news. The government’s task is to bridge that gap – which it has attempted through town-hall meetings and informational campaigns about energy bill support, wage increases, and so on. There’s evidence that once people see sustained lower inflation and perhaps an uptick in their take-home pay (helped by policies like expanding free childcare, which was introduced to reduce families’ costs), public sentiment will catch up. Until then, however, policy based on outcomes might not immediately translate into popularity. As an aide quipped, *“voters don’t carry spreadsheets”* – they might credit the government only when improvements are unmistakable in daily life.

**Immigration is yet another area where narratives diverge from facts.** Sensational media coverage of boat arrivals often gives the impression that the situation is chaotic and worsening (which, in fairness, it was in 2023–24). The current government has taken a more pragmatic, less bombastic approach than its predecessor: you won’t hear the PM using three-word slogans about migrants. Instead, they quietly worked on deals like the France “one in, one out” accord. This deal is *not* a flashy panacea and thus received somewhat muted coverage – aside from some corners of the press misrepresenting it as Britain “surrendering” (by agreeing to take in refugees from France). In truth, the arrangement is limited but innovative, creating a legal pathway and a return mechanism that could be built upon. It may not lend itself to triumphant soundbites, but on the ground it’s a step toward managing a complex problem. If over the next year the numbers of crossings begin to drop, it will validate this outcomes-focused strategy. Yet one can already predict that if crossings do fall, some media will claim credit goes to the *previous* government’s tough talk, while if crossings don’t fall, they’ll blame the current government’s “softness.” This is the kind of optics-versus-outcomes battle that policymakers constantly navigate. The current leadership has so far shown a willingness to endure some bruising headlines (and social media attacks) in pursuit of policies they believe will work in the long run – an approach the PM himself summarized as *“policy based on outcomes, not optics.”* 

In summary, the **media landscape** in Britain remains highly polarized, and public opinion can be influenced by headlines that don’t always align with reality. The first year of this government saw several instances where it had to make potentially unpopular choices – whether increasing spending to fix public services, cooperating with the EU, or taking a firm line on an international issue – in the belief that doing the right thing would yield results. Some of those results (ending strikes, re-engaging with Europe) are already evident; others (shorter hospital waits, more homes, fewer migrant crossings) will take longer to materialize. Patience and persistence are key. The government’s large parliamentary majority has given it the freedom to prioritize long-term outcomes over short-term polling hits[UK\-EU summit will be step on path to better relations, says Rachel Reeves | Europe | The Guardian](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2025/may/16/uk-eu-summit-will-be-step-on-path-to-better-relations-says-rachel-reeves). Indeed, one year on, many of its **“missions” are only just beginning to bear fruit** – progress is apparent in economic stability and restored alliances, while other pledges show only tiny steps forward. The coming years will reveal whether those seeds of progress can grow into genuine improvements felt by the public. For now, any fair assessment would conclude that **the record is mixed**: some early successes, several slow starts, and a few outright struggles. Crucially, however, the direction of travel has changed – with a focus on rebuilding capacity at home and credibility abroad – and that itself marks a meaningful break from the recent past. 

**Sources:** British media and government reports from 2024–2025 have been used to compile these findings, including analysis by the Institute for Government, Sky News Data & Forensics unit, Reuters, and The Guardian, among others[UK to recognise state of Palestine in September unless Israel holds to a ceasefire | Foreign policy | The Guardian](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2025/jul/29/uk-to-recognise-state-of-palestine-in-september-unless-israel-holds-to-a-ceasefire). These provide a data-backed picture of the government’s first-year outcomes versus its promises. The narrative in public discourse doesn’t always match the data, underscoring the importance of rigorous review like this in separating signal from noise. As the government moves into its second year, observers will be watching closely to see if modest trends turn into major shifts – and if the gap between perception and reality begins to narrow. The true test of these policies will be their **real-world impact on British people’s lives**, which ultimately is the metric that matters most.

# The War in Sudan (2023–Present): Comprehensive Overview

## Background and Causes  
Sudan’s 2023 civil war erupted from a power struggle between two wings of the country’s ruling military regime. General Abdel Fattah al-Burhan, commander of the Sudanese Armed Forces (SAF), and Lt. General Mohamed “Hemedti” Dagalo, leader of the Rapid Support Forces (RSF) paramilitary, were former allies who jointly toppled dictator Omar al-Bashir in 2019. However, tensions grew over plans to integrate the RSF into the regular army and transition to civilian rule. By early April 2023, RSF units deployed across Sudan and SAF troops massed in the capital Khartoum, amid failed negotiations over the chain of command. This deadlock exploded into open conflict on April 15, 2023, when heavy gunfire and explosions rocked Khartoum as the two factions clashed, each blaming the other for starting the fight. The involvement of foreign actors – for instance, weapons and support allegedly from Russia’s Wagner Group and the UAE to the RSF, and backing for the SAF from Egypt – further stoked the rivalry.

## Outbreak of War in April 2023  
When fighting broke out in Khartoum on April 15, 2023, battles quickly spread beyond the capital. The SAF and RSF fought pitched battles for control of strategic sites in Khartoum, including the army headquarters, the presidential palace, and the main airport. The capital region was soon split between the warring factions; General Burhan relocated the official government seat to Port Sudan on the Red Sea coast as RSF fighters entrenched themselves in parts of Khartoum. Simultaneously, violence ignited across other regions. In particular, the western region of Darfur – historically volatile after a 2003-2005 genocide – saw a resurgence of brutal attacks. The RSF, whose roots trace back to the notorious Janjaweed militia of Darfur, moved to seize major cities in Darfur amid the chaos. By late April 2023, reports emerged of escalating ethnic violence in West Darfur, raising alarms of history repeating itself.

## Escalation and Key Developments  
In the first weeks of the war, neither side delivered a decisive blow, and the conflict metastasized. Despite multiple internationally brokered ceasefires in 2023 (such as a **May 2023 Jeddah Declaration**), fighting persisted as each truce collapsed almost immediately. The RSF initially made rapid advances, taking control of much of **Khartoum** and large parts of **Darfur** by late 2023. Notably, **El Geneina**, the capital of West Darfur, fell into chaos with intense militia attacks. On June 14, 2023, the Governor of West Darfur, Khamis Abbakar, was assassinated just hours after he publicly accused the RSF and allied Arab militias of committing “genocide” in his state. His murder marked a grim turning point, symbolizing the collapse of law and order in Darfur. Throughout mid and late 2023, urban warfare raged in Khartoum (and its sister cities Omdurman and Bahri) while the Darfur region descended into factional bloodshed. By August 2023, the United Nations warned the conflict was “spiraling out of control” as violence spread and refugees poured out of the country.

Early 2024 brought new twists. The SAF managed to stabilize its defenses and even regain momentum on some fronts. In a coordinated offensive in late 2024, the army launched attacks to reclaim **Khartoum and surrounding areas**, gradually retaking territory from the RSF. By January 2025, Sudan’s military had driven RSF fighters out of key positions: for example, the SAF pushed the RSF out of most of **Omdurman**, recaptured a vital oil refinery near Khartoum, and reasserted control over almost all of **Bahri** (Khartoum North). In February 2025, the SAF also broke a two-year RSF siege of the city of **El Obeid** in North Kordofan, reopening a strategic rail link. However, the RSF retained a stronghold in **Darfur**, its home turf. Darfur’s largest city **El Fasher** remained fiercely contested; the RSF continued deadly assaults there and in other towns, often targeting non-Arab communities. Despite the SAF’s gains around the capital, **no side achieved a decisive victory**, and intense battles continued into 2025.

## Humanitarian Crisis and Displacement  
The war in Sudan has precipitated one of the world’s worst humanitarian crises. **Casualties** from direct violence are difficult to tally precisely; estimates vary widely. By late 2024, the Armed Conflict Location & Event Data Project (ACLED) had recorded at least 24,000 combat-related deaths, but many observers believe the true toll is much higher. In early 2025 a former U.S. Special Envoy for Sudan suggested the fighting may have killed up to **150,000 people** (including unreported victims) since April 2023. Moreover, countless Sudanese are dying indirectly from the war’s fallout – hunger, disease, and the collapse of healthcare. By one estimate, famine and related deprivation had already claimed over **half a million young children’s lives** in war-torn areas. Sudan is now considered the world’s worst humanitarian disaster, with approximately **25 million people** – over half the population – facing extreme hunger and malnutrition. Vital infrastructure has been wrecked: more than **80% of hospitals** in conflict areas are not functioning after being shelled, looted, or cut off from supplies.

The **displacement crisis** is staggering in scale. The war has uprooted at least **14 million people** from their homes in a little over two years, making it the world’s largest internal displacement crisis. As of early 2025, around **8.8 million** people were internally displaced within Sudan, and over **3.5 million** more had fled as refugees to neighboring countries. Families have streamed across borders into **Chad, South Sudan, Egypt, Ethiopia**, and the Central African Republic, where overstretched refugee camps have been overwhelmed. The United Nations reported that by August 2023, over **1 million** refugees had fled Sudan, while millions more were displaced inside the country. Today those numbers have only grown. Overall, humanitarian agencies estimate **30 million Sudanese** require urgent assistance for basic needs like food, water, and medical care. UN officials warn that Sudan is on the brink of the “world’s largest hunger crisis” due to the conflict’s disruption of farming and markets. Aid groups face immense challenges reaching people in need, as active combat, bureaucratic blockades, and looting of relief supplies by armed groups impede deliveries.

## Atrocities and War Crimes  
From the outset, the conflict in Sudan has been marked by egregious violence against civilians, drawing condemnation from human rights organizations. In **Darfur**, especially West Darfur, the RSF and allied Arab militias have perpetrated **ethnically targeted attacks** reminiscent of the Darfur genocide of the early 2000s. Villages of the Masalit and other non-Arab communities have been razed and civilians massacred. Human Rights Watch and other NGOs have documented numerous mass atrocities, including **large-scale killings, sexual violence, and the wholesale destruction of villages**. One particularly horrifying incident occurred in late October–early November 2023: RSF forces and their militia allies carried out a multi-day rampage in the town of **Ardamata** (West Darfur), slaughtering over **800 civilians** in an orgy of ethnically-driven killing. Such episodes underscore what observers have called a campaign of **ethnic cleansing** in parts of Darfur. In January 2024, the United Nations reported that an estimated **10,000–15,000 people** were killed in West Darfur in 2023 alone due to targeted ethnic violence by the RSF and its allies. The conflict has also seen widespread **sexual violence**: women and girls (some as young as 14) have been systematically raped and abused by fighters, according to evidence highlighted by the U.S. ambassador to the UN. These atrocities have prompted increasing international outcry. On **7 January 2025**, the United States government formally declared that the RSF’s attacks on non-Arab groups in Darfur constitute **genocide**, underscoring the severity of the crimes unfolding.

Abuses are not confined to one side. While the RSF and associated militias bear the brunt of international blame for deliberate attacks on civilians, the SAF has also been accused of serious violations. The army’s extensive use of **airstrikes and heavy artillery in urban centers** has caused substantial civilian casualties and infrastructure destruction. In Khartoum and Omdurman, indiscriminate shelling and air bombardments by SAF jets early in the war killed many non-combatants and devastated neighborhoods. Both sides have impeded humanitarian aid: there are reports of **looting of relief convoys** and bureaucratic obstruction by authorities, depriving civilians of lifesaving supplies. Such actions may amount to war crimes under international law. The International Criminal Court, which still has outstanding warrants against ex-dictator Bashir for past Darfur crimes, has signaled it is monitoring the current situation; Sudanese civil society and international activists are calling for accountability for the new wave of atrocities.

## International Response  
The international community’s response to Sudan’s war has been mixed, involving stalled diplomacy and growing humanitarian efforts. Early in the conflict, regional and global mediators tried to negotiate ceasefires. The **United States, Saudi Arabia, African Union, and United Nations** all pressed the warring generals to silence their guns. These efforts yielded multiple ceasefire announcements through 2023 – including an agreement in Jeddah in May 2023 – but none held on the ground. In March 2024, the UN Security Council unanimously passed a resolution demanding an immediate end to violence and unimpeded humanitarian access. A new round of talks was arranged shortly after, mediated by neighboring Libya and Turkey, but those **indirect negotiations** collapsed within days as the parties reneged and fighting persisted. Throughout 2024 and 2025, international envoys (from the UN, African and Arab leagues, the U.S., EU, etc.) continued to shuttle in search of a formula to stop the war, so far to little avail.

Humanitarian agencies and neighboring countries have been left to grapple with the fallout. The UN and NGOs like the World Food Programme, UNHCR, and the International Committee of the Red Cross have scaled up relief operations, though funding and access remain inadequate given the enormity of need. As one UN official put it, **“we are running out of time”** to prevent an even greater catastrophe. Appeals have been made for billions of dollars in aid, but donor response has been sluggish, complicated by global crises elsewhere. Meanwhile, Sudan’s neighbors worry about the war’s destabilizing effects: Chad, South Sudan, and Ethiopia face influxes of refugees and potential spillover of armed groups. Regional organizations (the **African Union, IGAD** East African bloc, and Arab League) have convened summits on Sudan, calling for a ceasefire and sometimes proposing peacekeeping missions, though none have materialized on the ground.

Several countries have directly influenced the conflict via support to the combatants. **Egypt** has openly backed the SAF (Burhan’s forces), concerned about stability on its southern border and wary of the RSF’s potential Islamist ties. Conversely, **the United Arab Emirates (UAE)** and, reportedly, **Russia (through the Wagner group)** have been accused of arming or funding the RSF, seeing Hemedti as an ally for their regional interests. This external meddling has drawn criticism: in fact, in March 2025 the Sudanese government (loyal to Burhan) filed a complaint at the International Court of Justice accusing the **UAE of complicity in genocide** by supplying weapons to the RSF. In response to the atrocities, Western nations have started to impose targeted **sanctions**. The United States, United Kingdom, European Union and others have announced sanctions on individuals and entities linked to both the RSF and SAF, citing **human rights abuses** and obstruction of peace. Notably, the U.S. sanctioned top RSF commanders (including Hemedti’s brother) for their role in the violence. These measures aim to pressure the warring leaders to negotiate, though so far the conflict rages on unabated.

## Current Status and Outlook  
As of mid-2025, the war in Sudan shows no definitive sign of resolution. Despite periods of stalemate and incremental shifts in territory, **fighting continues on multiple fronts**. The SAF controls most of the eastern regions and has re-established authority in central Sudan and parts of Khartoum, but the RSF remains entrenched in significant areas, especially in western Sudan. In **Darfur**, the RSF and allied militias still dominate most rural areas and several towns, perpetrating ongoing violence against communities perceived as pro-government. The capital Khartoum, though largely reclaimed by the army by early 2025, lies in ruins and is not completely secure. Intensive urban combat, looting, and lawlessness have driven the vast majority of Khartoum’s inhabitants to flee; the city’s population has plummeted as civilians shelter elsewhere or escape abroad. 

Diplomatically, there have been **renewed peace initiatives** in 2025 – including talks brokered by African states and behind-the-scenes communications between Burhan and Hemedti via intermediaries – but **no lasting ceasefire** has been achieved. Each side appears intent on a military solution, hoping to improve its bargaining position. The humanitarian situation is expected to further deteriorate if fighting drags on. Observers warn of **regional implications**: the protracted conflict threatens to destabilize the Horn of Africa and the Sahel, as arms flow across borders and ethnic strife spills into refugee communities. The outlook is exceedingly grim unless there is a dramatic turnaround. Sudan is essentially **fragmented** – with a de facto partition between SAF-held and RSF-held zones – and its economy and governance have collapsed. For millions of ordinary Sudanese, daily life is a struggle for survival amid shortages and violence. 

In summary, Sudan’s ongoing civil war, now well into its second year, has created a catastrophe of monumental proportions. All efforts to reconcile the rival factions have faltered so far. **Despite multiple negotiation attempts, no sustainable peace deal has emerged, and the war persists with devastating human cost and far-reaching consequences.** The international community faces an urgent challenge to ramp up humanitarian aid and renew pressure for a ceasefire. Without a resolution, the conflict will continue to exact an ever-growing toll on Sudan’s people and threaten stability across an already fragile region.
