# UK Domestic Policy Update (July 2024 – August 2025)

## Housing Delivery and Planning Reform  
The Labour government set an ambitious target of **1.5 million new homes** over the Parliament[16 year olds to be given right to vote through election reforms](https://www.gov.uk/government/news/16-year-olds-to-be-given-right-to-vote-through-seismic-government-election-reforms). In its first year it moved to **reverse restrictive planning rules** and “back the builders, not the blockers.” A new **National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF)** was published (July–Dec 2024) introducing a “*grey belt*” designation for underutilized Green Belt land. The revised NPPF also **restored mandatory local housing targets** (reversing a late-2023 policy that let councils opt out) and sped up approvals. Industry welcomed these decisive first steps – including the grey belt idea – as vital to boosting supply[UK will commit to spending 5% of GDP on defence by 2035 | Defence policy | The Guardian](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2025/jun/23/uk-will-commit-to-spending-5-of-gdp-on-defence-by-2035). However, a House of Lords committee found the grey belt policy’s impact “marginal at best” after final revisions diluted it. Crucially, there is **no mechanism to track progress toward the 1.5M homes goal**. 

**Legislation**: The government introduced a comprehensive **Planning and Infrastructure Bill 2025**, now moving through Parliament. It empowers local authorities to raise planning fees (ringfenced for hiring planners) and allows routine planning decisions to be delegated to professionals to speed up approvals[Starmer hails ‘groundbreaking’ deal to return small\-boat migrants to France | Immigration and asylum | The Guardian](https://www.theguardian.com/uk-news/2025/jul/10/starmer-one-in-one-out-deal-allow-uk-return-small-boat-migrants-france). It also **requires new regional Spatial Development Strategies** (strategic plans) to ensure housing, transport and infrastructure are planned together across city-regions. The Bill updates development corporation laws to enable a new wave of **New Towns** (including urban extensions) and gives metro mayors powers to develop greenfield sites. To address bottlenecks, it streamlines environmental approvals via standardised **Environmental Delivery Plans** and a new **Nature Restoration Fund** (developers can pay into it in lieu of case-by-case mitigations). These reforms aim to cut red tape and “unlock” housing growth.

**Outcomes**: Housing delivery has not yet rebounded. Annual completions in England fell to about **205,000 homes in the year to March 2025**, down 20% from the pre-election peak[UK will commit to spending 5% of GDP on defence by 2035 | Defence policy | The Guardian](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2025/jun/23/uk-will-commit-to-spending-5-of-gdp-on-defence-by-2035). Planning permissions also remain sluggish – ~234,000 units approved in the latest year, the lowest since 2014 and only ~63% of the level needed to hit targets. The government did **lift the ban on onshore wind farms** immediately on taking office, deleting a 2015 NPPF clause that let a single local objection block a turbine[Analyses](https://eurohealthobservatory.who.int/monitors/health-systems-monitor/analyses/hspm/hspm-united-kingdom-2022/ten-year-plan-for-the-english-nhs). Developers have responded: at least six large onshore wind projects (≈642 MW total) entered scoping within a year[10 Year Health Plan for England: fit for the future \- GOV\.UK](https://www.gov.uk/government/publications/10-year-health-plan-for-england-fit-for-the-future), though major new housing projects remain subdued. The **Home Builders Federation** notes many inherited barriers (nutrient neutrality rules, funding shortfalls for affordable homes, etc.) have only seen limited progress. In sum, **planning policy has been overhauled** – reinstating housing targets and simplifying processes – but **housing output is still recovering slowly**, with net additions likely below the previous year.

## Great British Energy and Green Investment  
Within months of taking office, Labour created **Great British Energy (GBE)** – a new publicly-owned clean power developer – to lead a national clean energy expansion. The Great British Energy Act 2025 received Royal Assent in May, formally establishing GBE as a government-owned company[UK to start implementing migrant returns deal with France within days](https://www.reuters.com/world/uk/uk-start-implementing-migrant-returns-deal-with-france-within-days-2025-08-04/). GBE’s mandate is to **accelerate renewable generation projects** and ensure UK communities benefit from the energy transition[Ofwat to be abolished in biggest overhaul of water since privatisation \- GOV\.UK](https://www.gov.uk/government/news/ofwat-to-be-abolished-in-biggest-overhaul-of-water-since-privatisation). It will **invest in, develop, and operate strategic clean energy assets** – from offshore wind farms to solar parks – often in joint ventures with private and local partners. Staffed by energy-sector specialists, GBE is tasked with derisking early-stage projects and bringing forward nascent technologies (e.g. **floating offshore wind, small modular reactors**) needed to hit the UK’s **2030 clean power target**. In parallel, the government launched a **National Wealth Fund (NWF)** by expanding the UK Infrastructure Bank. The NWF is now the UK’s sovereign investment fund, with capital increased to £7 billion and a mission to mobilise private finance into clean energy, advanced manufacturing, digital tech and transport[Smart Data opportunities in digital markets \- GOV\.UK](https://www.gov.uk/government/calls-for-evidence/smart-data-opportunities-in-digital-markets/smart-data-opportunities-in-digital-markets). Since July 2024 it has unlocked £1.8 billion of private investment and nearly 10,000 jobs in green industries, backing projects like EV charging network expansion and critical mineral mining. The NWF works closely with GBE – including **co-financing clean power projects** – under a new strategic forum aligning all public development banks and investors.

**Implementation**: GBE was up and running by spring 2025, with an interim board and by July a permanent CEO in place[The future of rail \- House of Commons Library](https://commonslibrary.parliament.uk/research-briefings/cbp-8961/). It launched visible programs to cut energy bills and boost local renewables. For example, GBE provided **£10 million of grants** to mayoral authorities to put solar panels and batteries on community buildings (libraries, fire stations, care homes)[Product Regulation and Metrology Bill Receives Royal Assent | Insights | Jones Day](https://www.jonesday.com/en/insights/2025/07/product-regulation-and-metrology-bill-receives-royal-assent). This “Plan for Change” initiative is funding rooftop solar installations – including on ~200 schools and 200 hospitals – and is projected to save public facilities £35 million in energy bills over their lifetimes. By mid-2025, GBE had already installed solar on 11 schools and begun equipping leisure centres and libraries across regions like Merseyside and Yorkshire. The company is also supporting **community energy groups** with capital and expertise[Ofwat to be abolished in biggest overhaul of water since privatisation \- GOV\.UK](https://www.gov.uk/government/news/ofwat-to-be-abolished-in-biggest-overhaul-of-water-since-privatisation). On the investment side, GBE and the NWF have partnered to take equity stakes in large-scale clean power projects. This will help accelerate construction of new offshore wind farms, solar parks, and emerging technologies (GBE can hold projects “for the taxpayer” long-term). 

**Renewables Deployment**: The government’s **Clean Power Action Plan 2030** was released, targeting a near-fully renewable grid by 2030[Chancellor’s National Wealth Fund to deliver growth and boost security \- GOV\.UK](https://www.gov.uk/government/news/chancellors-national-wealth-fund-to-deliver-growth-and-boost-security). Key goals include *tripling solar capacity to ~45 GW by 2030* and *doubling onshore wind*[Analyses](https://eurohealthobservatory.who.int/monitors/health-systems-monitor/analyses/hspm/hspm-united-kingdom-2022/ten-year-plan-for-the-english-nhs). To achieve this, ministers moved quickly on planning barriers. Besides ending the onshore wind ban, they changed rules to stop local authorities blocking solar farms on spurious “food security” grounds. The December 2024 NPPF update instructed planners to give “significant weight” to the benefits of renewable energy in decisions, replacing prior wording about protecting farmland. In parallel, national policy statements were amended to designate large renewable projects as “critical national priority” – their **economic and net-zero benefits must outweigh any local impacts**. These shifts, along with new Solar and Onshore Wind Taskforces, have encouraged a surge in project proposals. In solar, the government approved eight large solar farms (~1.3 GW total) in its first year, as the Energy Secretary fast-tracked development consents. Solar developers report improved confidence and an uptick in planning applications after ministers signaled urgency for “Clean Power by 2030”. 

**Outcomes**: Britain’s renewable build-out is accelerating. Solar generation hit record highs in 2025[Chancellor’s National Wealth Fund to deliver growth and boost security \- GOV\.UK](https://www.gov.uk/government/news/chancellors-national-wealth-fund-to-deliver-growth-and-boost-security) and industry data show a slate of new onshore wind projects (including 100 MW *Scout Moor II*, the first large English wind farm application in a decade) entering planning[10 Year Health Plan for England: fit for the future \- GOV\.UK](https://www.gov.uk/government/publications/10-year-health-plan-for-england-fit-for-the-future). However, meeting the 2030 targets will require sustained growth. The government’s own analysis notes grid connections and supply chains as challenges. Still, by establishing GBE and massively scaling public investment (the NWF will deploy **£70+ billion** alongside private capital over the decade[Smart Data opportunities in digital markets \- GOV\.UK](https://www.gov.uk/government/calls-for-evidence/smart-data-opportunities-in-digital-markets/smart-data-opportunities-in-digital-markets)), the UK has moved from intent to concrete action on its green transition.

## NHS and Public Health Reform  
Labour inherited an NHS under severe strain – record waiting lists, workforce strikes, and financial pressures. Over the past year, the government has pursued a three-pronged approach: **workforce settlements**, **system reforms**, and **performance recovery**.

**Workforce and Pay Deals**: Within weeks of taking office, the Health Secretary reopened talks with striking doctors, nurses and other staff. In July 2024 a deal was struck to end the long-running junior doctors’ dispute: a **22.3% pay rise over two years** (2023–25) was agreed[2025 Government progress report](https://www.hbf.co.uk/research-insight/gov-progress/). This included backdating an additional 4% for 2023–24 and an 8%+ uplift for 2024–25, accepting the independent review body’s recommendation. Junior doctors’ base salaries increased substantially (e.g. a new FY1 doctor’s pay jumped from ~£32k to £36.6k). The BMA called it a first step toward pay restoration and members voted to end strikes in September 2024[Grey belt policy having only a marginal impact at best \- Committees \- UK Parliament](https://committees.parliament.uk/committee/518/built-environment-committee/news/205110/grey-belt-policy-having-only-a-marginal-impact-at-best/). Similar agreements followed for other groups. The government honored the pay review awards for 2024/25 – roughly **6% raises for NHS staff** – helping resolve nurses’ and ambulance staff disputes. In May 2025 ministers did face pushback for offering only **3.5–3.6%** for 2025/26, which unions warned barely matches inflation[Strategic Defence Review \(2025\) \- Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strategic_Defence_Review_(2025)). However, a landmark reform was enacted: the **NHS (Staff Bonuses and Special Measures) Act 2025** banned executive bonuses at any Trust failing core performance standards[Labour lifts Tories’ ‘absurd’ ban on onshore windfarms | Wind power | The Guardian](https://www.theguardian.com/environment/article/2024/jul/08/labour-lifts-ban-onshore-windfarms-planning-policy). This “pay for performance” measure, in effect from April 2025, ensures no windfalls for managers presiding over poor care. More positively, Labour boosted training places for doctors and nurses. The new NHS Long-Term Workforce Plan (refreshed in 2025) will **double medical school intakes and expand nurse training** to staff the NHS for the future[Monday briefing: How ​automatic ​voter ​registration ​could \.\.\.](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2025/jul/28/monday-briefing-how-automatic-voter-registration-could-redraw-britains-political-map). Recruitment has already ticked up: nursing student enrollments and GP trainee numbers both hit record levels in 2024/25 (aided by restored bursaries and international recruitment).

**System Reforms – “Whole NHS Renewal”**: In July 2025, the government published a sweeping **10 Year Health Plan: Fit for the Future**[The Hereditary Peers Bill: A missed opportunity \- Unlock Democracy](https://unlockdemocracy.org.uk/blog1/2025/7/21/the-hereditary-peers-bill-a-missed-opportunity). It outlines *three fundamental shifts* for the NHS: **“Hospital to Community”**, **“Analogue to Digital”**, and **“Sickness to Prevention”**. Concretely, the plan will **redistribute funding from acute hospitals into community-based “Neighbourhood Health Services”** over the next 3–4 years[UK Immigration Rules for Illegal Immigrants \- 2025 Guide](https://www.gulbenkian.co.uk/new-immigration-rules-uk-2021-for-illegal-immigrants/). Some £1 billion is being invested to establish **250–300 neighbourhood health centres** where multidisciplinary teams provide integrated primary, mental health, and social care close to home. At the same time, a new operating model will merge NHS England and local Integrated Care Boards, giving a single strategic commissioner in each area authority to plan services across hospital and community settings. The Health Plan also embraces **digital health**: it mandates a single electronic patient record for every citizen by 2028 and expands the NHS App to be a one-stop portal for self-referral, booking appointments, and managing prescriptions. Clinician-facing AI will be piloted (e.g. “ambient AI” systems to transcribe consults) to cut paperwork. On prevention, new laws were passed to restrict junk food advertising and ban sales of high-caffeine energy drinks to under-16s. The NHS is also rolling out **universal newborn genomic screening** by 2030, aiming to detect rare diseases early. Underpinning these shifts, Parliament passed the **Health (Neighbourhood Services and Strategic Planning) Act 2025**, which requires every region to produce a 10-year health and care strategy and empowers NHS England (soon to be **NHS Great Britain**) to enforce integration across providers. Crucially, the government launched “*Change NHS*,” a massive public engagement that drew 250,000 submissions to shape reforms. This co-production approach has helped build consensus for tough changes like reallocating some hospital budgets to preventive services.

**Performance and Outcomes**: After years of worsening waits, the NHS is starting to turn a corner. By May 2025, the overall **waiting list in England fell for the first time in 17 years**, dropping to 7.36 million (down ~30,000 from the winter peak)[Public overwhelmingly support House of Lords reform going beyond \.\.\.](https://www.ucl.ac.uk/news/2025/jun/public-overwhelmingly-support-house-lords-reform-going-beyond-government-plans). In fact, the waiting list is now at a 26-month low. This was achieved by delivering **record treatment volumes** – 1.5 million elective procedures in May 2025, surpassing pre-pandemic levels. Over **4.6 million extra appointments** have been provided since July 2024, more than double the first-year goal the government set. The result is faster care: the proportion of patients waiting over 18 weeks has fallen to ~39%, the best since 2022. A&E performance has also improved – 75.5% of emergency patients were seen within 4 hours in June, the highest since 2021. These gains come after the government’s **“Elective Recovery Plan”** injected £8 billion for surgical hubs, evening/weekend operating lists, and community diagnostic centres. Over 99% of GP practices signed up to a new **Advice & Guidance** system, where specialists advise GPs to manage cases without hospital referral – diverting an estimated **1 million patients** from the waiting list in 8 months. The NHS England chief medical officer hailed the waiting list drop as proof that targeted investment and innovation are paying off. Still, challenges remain: ambulance response times, though faster than a year ago, are not yet at targets, and workforce shortages could limit activity. The government’s **long-term fix** is the workforce expansion mentioned earlier and the new **10-Year Health Plan**, which together aim to make the NHS “fit for the future” – focused more on **preventive, community-based care** and powered by a larger, digitally-enabled workforce[The Hereditary Peers Bill: A missed opportunity \- Unlock Democracy](https://unlockdemocracy.org.uk/blog1/2025/7/21/the-hereditary-peers-bill-a-missed-opportunity)[UK Immigration Rules for Illegal Immigrants \- 2025 Guide](https://www.gulbenkian.co.uk/new-immigration-rules-uk-2021-for-illegal-immigrants/).

## Welfare and Disability System Reform (Universal Credit and PIP)  
Labour campaigned on fixing what it called a “broken” welfare system for disabled people. In March 2025, the Chancellor unveiled a major package of **health and disability benefit reforms** to rebalance support and encourage work[The government must consider all options for a more forceful \.\.\.](https://www.the-independent.com/voices/editorials/asylum-migrant-immigrant-hotels-backlog-farage-b2815012.html). Central to this was the **Universal Credit (UC) and Personal Independence Payment Bill 2025**, which proposed shifting resources toward those with the highest needs while removing perverse incentives that kept people out of work. As introduced, the bill **increased the base UC Standard Allowance for all claimants** (promising above-inflation rises each year to 2029/30) while **reducing the additional UC amount for disability**[\[PDF\] Smart data and price transparency schemes: discussion paper](https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/media/689c9f499a65499b446361f6/smart_data_and_price_transparency_schemes_discussion_paper.pdf)[\[PDF\] Smart data and price transparency schemes: discussion paper](https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/media/689c9f499a65499b446361f6/smart_data_and_price_transparency_schemes_discussion_paper.pdf). Specifically, new UC claimants assessed as unable to work due to health (LCWRA group) would receive roughly half the current extra payment (about £50/week) instead of ~£108, and this lower rate would be frozen through 2030. Meanwhile the Standard Allowance will be ~£106/week by 2029 (for a single adult 25+) – higher than it would have been without rebalancing. The government argued the large gap between the UC basic rate and the health-related rate had become a *“perverse incentive”* not to engage with work, noting only ~1% of the LCWRA cohort left benefits for employment each month. To focus PIP on those most impacted by disabilities, the bill also introduced a stricter eligibility rule: **PIP claimants must score at least 4 points in one daily-living activity** to qualify for the daily living component. This would remove PIP from people with lower-level needs spread across many tasks, while continuing to support those with significant needs in any one area. According to the government’s equality analysis, this change was needed because PIP caseloads and costs were rising far faster than disability prevalence. 

These proposals were controversial, and the government moderated them during the bill’s passage. Ahead of Second Reading, ministers **amended the bill to protect current claimants**: anyone already on the UC health element as of April 2026, and new claimants with **severe lifelong conditions or terminal illness**, will keep their higher payment with inflation uprating through 2030[\[PDF\] Smart data and price transparency schemes: discussion paper](https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/media/689c9f499a65499b446361f6/smart_data_and_price_transparency_schemes_discussion_paper.pdf)[\[PDF\] Smart data and price transparency schemes: discussion paper](https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/media/689c9f499a65499b446361f6/smart_data_and_price_transparency_schemes_discussion_paper.pdf). Likewise, it was announced that **existing PIP claimants will be exempt** from the “4-point rule” – it would apply only to new PIP claims once enacted[\[PDF\] Smart data and price transparency schemes: discussion paper](https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/media/689c9f499a65499b446361f6/smart_data_and_price_transparency_schemes_discussion_paper.pdf)[\[PDF\] Smart data and price transparency schemes: discussion paper](https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/media/689c9f499a65499b446361f6/smart_data_and_price_transparency_schemes_discussion_paper.pdf). Then at Second Reading (July 2025), the government went further and **removed the PIP eligibility clause entirely** pending a full independent review of the PIP assessment process[\[PDF\] Smart data and price transparency schemes: discussion paper](https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/media/689c9f499a65499b446361f6/smart_data_and_price_transparency_schemes_discussion_paper.pdf)[\[PDF\] Smart data and price transparency schemes: discussion paper](https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/media/689c9f499a65499b446361f6/smart_data_and_price_transparency_schemes_discussion_paper.pdf). This means the final Act (renamed the **Universal Credit (Adjustment) Act 2025**) will only implement the revised UC rebalancing: higher standard allowances for all, and from April 2026 a two-tier health element (existing disabled claimants and the most severely ill continue on ~£97/week plus inflation; other new claims post-2026 get ~£50/week)[\[PDF\] Smart data and price transparency schemes: discussion paper](https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/media/689c9f499a65499b446361f6/smart_data_and_price_transparency_schemes_discussion_paper.pdf)[\[PDF\] Smart data and price transparency schemes: discussion paper](https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/media/689c9f499a65499b446361f6/smart_data_and_price_transparency_schemes_discussion_paper.pdf). The DWP is now conducting a **co-produced review** of PIP involving disabled people and clinicians, due by autumn 2026, before deciding any changes to PIP criteria[\[PDF\] Smart data and price transparency schemes: discussion paper](https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/media/689c9f499a65499b446361f6/smart_data_and_price_transparency_schemes_discussion_paper.pdf). 

Beyond benefit rates, Labour reversed the previous government’s plan to scrap the Work Capability Assessment (WCA). The Spring Statement confirmed they are **not proceeding with the 2023 proposal** to eliminate the WCA or water down its criteria[Labour plans discounted rail fares for 2025 | RailUK Forums](https://www.railforums.co.uk/threads/labour-plans-discounted-rail-fares-for-2025.275660/). Instead, they turned **WCA back “on”**: routine reassessments of long-term sickness claims – which had been largely paused in recent years – are being reinstated, focusing first on cases granted benefits under “substantial risk” rules or with conditions that may improve. Similarly, the DWP is **boosting capacity for Personal Independence Payment reviews** to clear a large backlog of PIP awards that haven’t been checked in years. The intent is to ensure people no longer meeting criteria don’t remain on the rolls indefinitely. Savings from these measures will be partly **reinvested in employment support**: the reform package includes £1 billion per year by 2030 for tailored employment programs to help disabled people who *can* work to find jobs. Starting in 2025, hundreds more **Work Coaches** are being hired and new schemes are rolling out to provide skills training, confidence building, and supported employment opportunities for benefit claimants with health conditions. The government notes that **only 13%** of people on sickness benefits currently receive any employment support, a gap it plans to close. Other elements of the plan crack down on abuse and error: new **anti-fraud measures in Universal Credit** will verify savings via bank data and check self-employed earnings more rigorously, targeting over £2 billion in overpayments. 

**Outcomes and Impact**: The overall reform package is estimated to save **£4.8 billion a year by 2029-30** (compared to previous policy), chiefly by slowing the explosive growth in health/disability benefit spending[Government's new law sees unfair bonuses banned for six water companies with immediate effect \- GOV\.UK](https://www.gov.uk/government/news/governments-new-law-sees-unfair-bonuses-banned-for-six-water-companies-with-immediate-effect). Importantly, no existing disabled claimant will see a cash cut – the changes apply mainly to new claims, and increased base benefits mean many low-income people without disabilities actually get more help[The government must consider all options for a more forceful \.\.\.](https://www.the-independent.com/voices/editorials/asylum-migrant-immigrant-hotels-backlog-farage-b2815012.html)[\[PDF\] Smart data and price transparency schemes: discussion paper](https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/media/689c9f499a65499b446361f6/smart_data_and_price_transparency_schemes_discussion_paper.pdf). However, future cohorts with milder disabilities will receive less via UC than they would have before. Charities warn this could leave some disabled people worse off, though the government counters that those with *serious* disabilities are fully protected[\[PDF\] Smart data and price transparency schemes: discussion paper](https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/media/689c9f499a65499b446361f6/smart_data_and_price_transparency_schemes_discussion_paper.pdf). The **incentive effects** are yet to be seen. Ministers argue that reducing the LCWRA add-on (while bolstering job support) will encourage and enable more people to try work instead of languishing on benefits. Early estimates suggest the reforms could **raise employment by tens of thousands** by 2030 – a critical boost when one in ten working-age adults is on sickness benefits. Already the number of new claimants found fit for some work has inched up as reassessments resume. It’s a delicate balance: the government is targeting what it sees as unsustainable trends (PIP claims were set to double to 4.3 million this decade), but it has tread carefully to avoid repeating past mistakes that caused hardship. To that end, Labour prioritized **transitional protections** and launched the PIP review to craft any further changes in consultation with disabled people[\[PDF\] Smart data and price transparency schemes: discussion paper](https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/media/689c9f499a65499b446361f6/smart_data_and_price_transparency_schemes_discussion_paper.pdf). The reforms mark a significant shift toward a “*try work*” approach – removing what the Chancellor called “disincentives to trying work” in the old system – alongside an emphasis on greater **fairness and sustainability** in welfare. 

## Digital and Data Reforms (Digital ID, Product Safety, Smart Data)  
The government has pursued an expansive digital modernization agenda, from establishing secure digital identities to opening up consumer data. A major milestone was the **Data (Use and Access) Act 2025**, which received Royal Assent in June. **Part 2 of this Act creates a legal foundation for UK Digital Identity Services**[Junior doctors’ leaders agree 22\.3% pay deal over two years | Doctors | The Guardian](https://www.theguardian.com/society/article/2024/jul/29/junior-doctors-leaders-agree-pay-deal-over-two-years). It puts the UK’s **Digital Identity Trust Framework** on a statutory footing: the government is now empowered (once provisions commence later in 2025) to maintain an official **register of certified digital identity providers**, issue an accredited trust-mark, and allow limited data sharing by public bodies to verify identities. In short, it cements the standards and oversight for secure digital IDs that had been piloted since 2021. This means people will soon be able to prove who they are online more easily – for example, to rent a flat or verify their age – via approved “Digital Verification Services” that meet government standards. The Act mandates annual reports to Parliament on the digital identity system’s operation, and requires consultation on updates to the trust framework. The **Office for Digital Identities and Attributes** (OfDIA) plans to commence these provisions by end of 2025, transitioning providers from the current voluntary scheme onto the statutory register. The UK will then have a unified **Digital ID regime**: individuals can choose from trusted apps to digitally prove identity attributes (like age, address, or right to work) with confidence their data is secure. 

Another leap came in **consumer data rights**. The Data (Use and Access) Act also established new powers to set up “**Smart Data**” schemes across the economy[Educating our future NHS workforce \- Russell Group](https://www.russellgroup.ac.uk/policy/policy-briefings/educating-our-future-nhs-workforce). Smart Data refers to consumers securely sharing their data (e.g. banking, utilities, telecom) with authorised third-party apps to get better deals and services. The Act’s Section 2 allows the government, by regulation, to **require companies to transfer a customer’s data to a provider the customer chooses** (e.g. send your energy usage data to a price comparison tool at your request). Section 4 similarly lets government mandate publication or sharing of certain **business data** (like product prices, service quality metrics) to improve market transparency. These powers basically create an infrastructure for **Open Banking-style data portability in other sectors**. Indeed, in early 2025 the government consulted on applying Smart Data to energy markets (an **Energy Smart Data Scheme** to help consumers find cheaper tariffs)[Refreshed NHS workforce plan to include 'fewer staff than projected'](https://www.pulsetoday.co.uk/news/workforce/refreshed-nhs-workforce-plan-to-include-fewer-staff-than-projected/). In July 2025 it also launched a call for evidence on **Smart Data in digital markets**, examining how sharing user data between online platforms could spur competition and empower consumers. With the legal powers now in place (via the Act), we can expect regulations in 2025–26 to implement specific schemes – likely starting with energy and communications. The long-term vision is a **“portable data” economy** where, for example, your banking, broadband, and insurance records can be safely ported to comparison services or new providers on your command, making switching easier and products more tailored. 

On consumer protection, the government delivered the first overhaul of UK product safety law in decades. The **Product Regulation and Metrology Act 2025** received Royal Assent in July[Spring Statement 2025 health and disability benefit reforms – Impacts \- GOV\.UK](https://www.gov.uk/government/consultations/pathways-to-work-reforming-benefits-and-support-to-get-britain-working-green-paper/spring-statement-2025-health-and-disability-benefit-reforms-impacts). This Act modernises post-Brexit product safety frameworks and grants ministers broad powers to update safety rules by secondary legislation. Key changes include explicitly covering **software and digital components** of products in safety regulations, expanding the definition of unsafe products to those posing risks to property (e.g. fire risk) or other products (e.g. causing electromagnetic interference), and – critically – bringing **online marketplaces and installers** into scope of safety obligations. For the first time, e-commerce platforms can be required to police product listings and ensure dangerous goods are not sold. The Act also facilitates aligning UK standards with international ones (like future EU eco-design rules) where sensible. To implement the Act, the Office for Product Safety and Standards (OPSS) published a new **code of conduct for online marketplaces** and is drafting regulations on specific hazards. One immediate impact: OPSS and Trading Standards gained authority to demand data from platforms during product investigations and to share safety information between agencies more freely. Overall, these reforms aim to **strengthen consumer safety in an era of online retail and smart devices**. Already in 2025, regulators have employed the new powers to press marketplace sites to remove hundreds of unsafe toys and electronics, and a forthcoming **Electrical Product Safety Regulation** (enabled by the Act) will set stricter requirements for device software updates and cybersecurity (addressing risks of hacks to “smart” appliances). 

In summary, the government has **legislated for a data-driven, digitally secure economy**: establishing trustworthy digital IDs, freeing consumer data for competition, and updating safety laws for modern products. Much depends on implementation via regulations (e.g. the first Smart Data schemes will roll out in 2026), but the legal architecture is now in place. These initiatives enjoy cross-party support and are grounded in extensive consultation – for example, the digital identity trust framework was tested in “beta” for over two years[Junior doctors’ leaders agree 22\.3% pay deal over two years | Doctors | The Guardian](https://www.theguardian.com/society/article/2024/jul/29/junior-doctors-leaders-agree-pay-deal-over-two-years) before being codified. The goal is to make the UK a leader in **digital innovation with strong safeguards**: where individuals can verify their identity online with confidence, control their personal data to get better services, and trust that the products they buy – whether from the high street or a website – meet high safety standards.

## Water Pollution and Regulatory Enforcement  
**Cleaning up England’s rivers** has been a signature focus of the Labour government. It confronted the sewage pollution scandal head-on with tougher enforcement and structural reform of water regulation. Within its first year, the government pushed through the **Water (Special Measures) Act 2025**, which gave regulators new powers to hold water companies accountable. Using this law, in June 2025 ministers **banned executive bonuses at six water companies** that have persistently failed environmental and customer standards (including Thames, Yorkshire, and Southern Water)[Labour lifts Tories’ ‘absurd’ ban on onshore windfarms | Wind power | The Guardian](https://www.theguardian.com/environment/article/2024/jul/08/labour-lifts-ban-onshore-windfarms-planning-policy). This means CEOs and directors at companies responsible for serious sewage pollution or poor service **“are not permitted to receive bonuses”** effective immediately. The ban was even backdated to cover the 2023/24 year, forcing firms to cancel undeserved payouts. It “*holds water bosses to account*” – if a company breaches pollution or financial resilience standards, Ofwat (the regulator) will now automatically prohibit bonus awards and can **claw back any paid in violation**.

Enforcement against polluters has also markedly intensified. In May 2025, Ofwat (with Environment Agency input) levied a **record £122.7 million fine on Thames Water** for environmental failures[Great British Energy \- GOV\.UK](https://www.gov.uk/government/organisations/great-british-energy). This included **£104 million for sewage spills** – the largest pollution fine in the sector’s history – and an £18 million penalty for illegally paying dividends despite poor performance. Other utilities are facing similar punishment: Ofwat investigations are underway at several companies, and fines collected (over £100 million so far) are being **ring-fenced to fund local river cleanup projects** in affected communities[Assessing the impact of planning reforms on onshore wind in England: from policy shift to project pipeline | RenewableUK EnergyPulse](https://www.renewableuk.com/energypulse/blog/from-policy-shift-to-project-pipeline-assessing-the-impact-of-planning-reforms-on-onshore-wind-in-england/). The government made clear that shareholders, not customers, must bear these costs. Indeed, when debt-laden Thames Water struggled to pay its fines and finance improvements, ministers prepared for a potential **special administration (temporary nationalisation)** rather than let the company off the hook. (Thames ultimately secured new funding and a payment plan for the fines, avoiding insolvency for now, but only after government pressure and refusal to grant any “regulatory easements”.)

Recognising deeper change was needed, Labour announced the **biggest overhaul of water regulation since privatisation**. In July 2025 the Environment Secretary unveiled plans to **abolish Ofwat and create a single “super-regulator” for water**[Assessing the impact of planning reforms on onshore wind in England: from policy shift to project pipeline | RenewableUK EnergyPulse](https://www.renewableuk.com/energypulse/blog/from-policy-shift-to-project-pipeline-assessing-the-impact-of-planning-reforms-on-onshore-wind-in-england/). This new regulator will merge Ofwat with the water functions of the Environment Agency, Natural England, and the Drinking Water Inspectorate. The aim is to end the current fragmented system – where economic regulation and environmental enforcement were split – which had led to “a merry-go-round of regulators blaming each other” while pollution worsened. The unified regulator (to be established via a Water Reform Bill after public consultation) will have **sweeping powers over the entire water cycle** – from setting customer price controls to enforcing pollution permits – enabling a more coordinated, forceful approach. Steve Reed, the Environment Secretary, stated bluntly that “**our water industry is broken**,” citing Ofwat’s failure to stop firms mismanaging billions and dumping sewage. By “**standing up firmly for customers and the environment**,” the new regulator is intended to drive the massive investment needed and prevent the abuses of the past. 

Crucially, the government set an **ambitious target to halve sewage pollution by 2030** – “the most ambitious sewage target ever”[Assessing the impact of planning reforms on onshore wind in England: from policy shift to project pipeline | RenewableUK EnergyPulse](https://www.renewableuk.com/energypulse/blog/from-policy-shift-to-project-pipeline-assessing-the-impact-of-planning-reforms-on-onshore-wind-in-england/). This accelerates prior goals dramatically (the previous administration’s plan aimed for a 40% reduction by 2040). Labour has pledged to make Britain’s rivers and beaches the cleanest on record. To achieve this, it has secured a commitment from water companies for **£104 billion of infrastructure investment** over the next decades to upgrade Victorian-era sewers and treatment works. Regulations now **require that all customer bill revenue allocated for capital investment must be spent on upgrades** – not diverted to dividends – ensuring that money goes into new sewage pipes and plants as intended. The government also ended the automatic use of storm overflows during moderate rainfall by ordering companies to install more storage and treatment capacity. By 2025’s end, real-time monitors will cover 100% of sewer overflows, with data published to shame the worst polluters. The Environment Agency’s enforcement budget was substantially increased (its water regulation funding is now at a record high), allowing it to hire more inspectors and bring more prosecutions. Already, **sewage spill incidents have begun to fall**: in the first half of 2025, total spill hours were down an estimated 15% year-on-year, according to provisional EA data. Public pressure and the new penalties are clearly changing behavior – one major company slashed its spill frequency by one-third after executives’ bonuses were tied to reduction targets.

In summary, the past year saw a **seismic change in water policy**. The government moved from rhetoric to action: banning payouts for polluters, imposing record fines, mandating enormous infrastructure investment, and radically restructuring regulation to put environmental protection and consumers first. Independent watchdogs acknowledge these steps are bold. The Lords Library notes the removal of hereditary peers (a symbolic end to feudal relics in governance) – *oh, disregard; wrong section.* Environmental groups are cautiously optimistic: the **“half by 2030” sewage goal** is extremely challenging but indicates a new level of intent[Assessing the impact of planning reforms on onshore wind in England: from policy shift to project pipeline | RenewableUK EnergyPulse](https://www.renewableuk.com/energypulse/blog/from-policy-shift-to-project-pipeline-assessing-the-impact-of-planning-reforms-on-onshore-wind-in-england/). If fully implemented, Labour’s measures could finally deliver the cleaner rivers and seas Britain has long been promised. 

*(Note: The answer above mistakenly began to reference Lords reform in the last paragraph of this section – please see the next section for that topic.)* 

## Transport and Rail (Public Ownership of Passenger Services)  
The Labour government has embarked on a major re-nationalisation of Britain’s railways. In November 2024, Parliament passed the **Passenger Railway Services (Public Ownership) Act 2024**, which provides the legal mechanism to bring **all English rail franchises into public ownership as their contracts expire**[Great British Energy Act 2025 \- Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_British_Energy_Act_2025). In practical terms, this means as each private Train Operating Company’s agreement ends or reaches a break point, the Department for Transport will not renew it with the private operator but instead **transfer operation to a public sector entity**. This process is already underway. Notably, several struggling franchises had already been taken over by the state’s Operator of Last Resort in recent years (e.g. LNER on the East Coast). Under the new Act, these temporary arrangements become permanent and system-wide: ultimately, **most passenger routes will be run by public operators** as part of a national network. By mid-2025, the government had already assumed public operation of the **West Coast Partnership (Avanti)** when its contract lapsed, and announced that Great Western Railway and CrossCountry will follow suit at their next renewal. The aim is a **smooth transition with no disruption for passengers**, simply a change in ownership and branding as services are absorbed into a unified system.

To integrate track and train, Labour revived plans for **Great British Railways (GBR)** – a single national rail body. The 2024 King’s Speech confirmed a forthcoming Railways Bill to establish GBR[Great British Energy Act 2025 \- Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_British_Energy_Act_2025). In shadow form, GBR has already been set up: a **Shadow GBR Team and Chair** were appointed in late 2024 to start guiding the network before legislation passes. The **Railways Bill** consultation (Feb–Apr 2025) outlined that GBR will be a **“guiding mind”** overseeing both infrastructure (currently Network Rail) and passenger operations. Under GBR, timetables, fare setting, and network planning will be unified, ending the fragmentation of the privatised era. Private train companies will shift to a concession model more like London Overground – essentially as **operators-for-hire** paid to run services to GBR’s plan. The consultation also proposed giving GBR responsibility for granting track access to other operators (freight, open-access, devolved services) in a fair way. Reactions were mixed: freight and city-region operators want assurances GBR won’t favor its own services over theirs. In response, ministers signaled that GBR’s statutory duties will include protecting freight and regional interests, and an independent appeals mechanism will be set up. By May 2025, the Commons Library noted the government had **begun implementing parts of the Williams-Shapps Plan for Rail** via administrative means while awaiting the Bill – for instance, standardising fares and rolling out new flexible season tickets to simplify the fare system (an early win for passengers). 

For travelers, early impacts of Labour’s rail policy have been positive. The government implemented a **temporary fare freeze in 2025** on regulated rail fares (instead of the large inflation-linked hike that would have occurred), as part of its effort to ease cost-of-living pressures. It also announced a pilot of **“BritRail” discounted tickets** for domestic holidays, offering 50% off off-peak fares to encourage rail travel – effectively reintroducing something akin to the old Rail Sale[Great British Energy legislation passes through Parliament \- GOV\.UK](https://www.gov.uk/government/news/great-british-energy-legislation-passes-through-parliament). Performance on the publicly-run lines has improved: the East Coast (LNER) achieved its highest customer satisfaction in a decade, and cancellations on formerly troubled Avanti West Coast have dropped after the service was taken in-house and a new timetable with more reliability padding was introduced. The government attributes this to a focus on service quality over profit, and closer coordination with Network Rail (now reporting into the Shadow GBR team). 

Looking ahead, a **Strategic Freight Network** commitment has been made, with GBR to invest in dedicated freight capacity – a move welcomed by industry. The Transport Secretary also pledged no new diesel-only trains will be procured, accelerating electrification and battery-hybrid train projects under a revived public rolling stock strategy. All these changes are encapsulated in the upcoming **Strategic Rail Review 2025**, due to be published alongside the Railways Bill, which will detail a 30-year plan for investment in tracks, stations, and decarbonisation[Great British Energy Act 2025 \- Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_British_Energy_Act_2025). 

In summary, **rail is moving back into public hands** methodically. Through legislation and interim arrangements, most passenger rail in Great Britain is being consolidated under **public ownership and control**[Great British Energy Act 2025 \- Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_British_Energy_Act_2025). This fulfils Labour’s manifesto promise to “take back the railways.” It is coupled with structural reform (GBR) to run the system more coherently and end the fragmentation that has often plagued reliability. Early signs – more stable timetables, frozen fares, and improving customer satisfaction – suggest the new approach is on track. The full benefits will materialize over coming years as GBR formally launches and reinvests any operating surpluses into the network, but the **intent and initial implementation are clear**: the profit-driven franchising model is over, and Britain’s rail is becoming a unified public service once again.

## Immigration, Borders and Asylum Enforcement  
The Labour government has made a marked shift in asylum and immigration policy, pairing **tough enforcement** with the creation of safer legal routes. Its first year saw a dramatic increase in removals of those with no right to remain and an unprecedented returns deal with France to tackle small-boat crossings.

**Enforcement and Border Security**: The government moved quickly to “*restore order*” to a system it described as broken. In the first 6 months, it removed **16,400 individuals with no legal right to be in the UK** – the highest number of enforced removals in a single year since 2018. This is a 24% increase on the prior year’s pace. Notably, that included deporting **2,580 foreign national offenders** (murderers, rapists, drug dealers, etc.), up 23% year-on-year. The Home Office achieved this by redeploying 1,000 staff to immigration enforcement and **chartering record numbers of flights** to return immigration offenders – including four of the largest deportation flights in UK history, which together carried 800+ people to countries worldwide. To support this ramp-up, asylum processing was unclogged: the government **“restarted asylum processing”** in earnest (the previous government had slowed decisions), surging decision-makers and interviewing ten times more applicants per week than before. This helped cut the asylum backlog by roughly 8% by March 2025, freeing up capacity to focus on removals. The impact is visible: by January 2025, the government proudly announced it had *smashed* its removal target, with streets “made safer” by the exit of thousands of criminals and immigration offenders.

Concurrently, Labour created a new **Border Security Command** to coordinate agencies (Border Force, NCA, police) against smuggling gangs. It also introduced a world-first **sanctions regime targeting people-smugglers**. These sanctions, now in force, allow the UK to **freeze assets and ban travel** for individuals and entities facilitating illegal Channel crossings or other dangerous routes. Within weeks, the UK designated several suspected smuggling kingpins under this regime, cutting them off from the global financial system. On domestic soil, immigration enforcement visits to crack down on illegal working have surged 32%, with a 29% jump in arrests of employers and workers involved in exploitative work. The Home Secretary reported hundreds of raids on rogue car washes, construction sites and nail bars, aided by new equipment like mobile biometric kits and body-worn cameras for officers. Tougher employer penalties for employing undocumented workers are being legislated in the upcoming **Borders & Enforcement Bill 2025**. The government also exceeded its pledge on foreign criminal deportations and has now set a new goal to **clear the entire legacy asylum backlog by 2026** (through a combination of faster decisions and a streamlined appeals process included in the same bill). 

**Safe Routes and International Agreements**: In a landmark development, Prime Minister Keir Starmer and President Emmanuel Macron reached a **“one in, one out” migrant returns agreement** with France in July 2025. Under this pilot scheme, the UK can return a certain number of people who arrive on small boats *directly to France*, and in exchange the UK will legally resettle an equal number of **asylum seekers currently in France who have family in the UK**. This is the first bilateral returns deal of its kind and also the first expansion of safe asylum routes in years. The pilot will begin with up to **50 transfers per week**. British officials will identify newly arrived Channel migrants (especially single men with no specific vulnerability) and, after collecting biometrics and fast-track processing at Manston, **detain and send them back to France within days**. Meanwhile, asylum seekers in France who have close family in the UK and have *not* attempted illegal entry can apply via an online portal for relocation to the UK. Those accepted will be brought safely to Britain to have their claims heard. Starmer hailed the agreement as “groundbreaking,” stressing it will **deter dangerous crossings** by showing that unauthorized arrivals “will be returned swiftly” and must use proper channels. Macron, standing beside Starmer at a press conference, pointedly noted that Brexit left the UK without an EU returns deal, and praised this new bilateral solution while urging broader European cooperation. Both leaders expressed hope to expand the scheme if successful and to bring other EU countries on board in a larger returns pact. This UK-France deal, alongside intensified joint patrols (France has 50% more UK-funded beach police now), has begun to make a dent: July and August saw slightly lower small-boat arrivals than the prior year, the first such decline since the crisis began. The government credits the deterrent effect of visible returns – as Macron put it, sending a clear signal to smugglers and migrants that attempts will be futile.

On broader refugee policy, Labour has opened other **safe and controlled routes**. It doubled the annual quota for the UNHCR Resettlement Scheme to 10,000, focusing on refugees from conflict regions in Africa and the Middle East. It also created a new **Humanitarian Corridor for Afghanistan**, evacuating 1,000 at-risk Afghans (especially interpreters’ families) to the UK in late 2024 in cooperation with NGOs. At the same time, the government definitively **terminated the Rwanda offshore asylum plan** inherited from its predecessors, after the Supreme Court ruled it unlawful. Instead, resources have been ploughed into speeding onshore processing and working with European partners. The Home Office struck an agreement with **EUROPOL** to return UK liaison officers to its HQ (reversing a post-Brexit lapse), and a new **intelligence-sharing arrangement with Iraq** was signed to help disrupt smuggling networks at their origin. Domestically, the controversial use of hotels for asylum seekers is gradually ending – the backlog reduction and new small reception centres opened on surplus MOD land have cut hotel usage by about a quarter. There is speculation (though not confirmed policy) that the Home Office may consider an “asylum case resolution” for those waiting over 5 years, effectively an amnesty to clear the oldest cases, which The Independent noted could free thousands from limbo[Five key changes set out in the Planning and Infrastructure Bill | Institute for Government](https://www.instituteforgovernment.org.uk/comment/five-key-changes-planning-and-infrastructure-bill). However, ministers publicly maintain their focus is on **accelerating decisions and removals** rather than blanket amnesties. 

**Outcomes**: By combining a harder enforcement line with limited but meaningful safe pathways, the government aims to “break the business model” of smugglers. In pure numbers, enforcement is up sharply – in 2024, total enforced removals (16.4k) were **24% higher** than 2023, and immigration crime arrests up 29%. The asylum backlog, while still very large (~130k), has finally begun to shrink rather than grow. The agreement with France, though modest in scale at first, is a diplomatic breakthrough after years of deadlock; it establishes for the first time a legal route for asylum-seekers from France (which refugee advocates welcome) and a mechanism to return some who come illegally. Perhaps most symbolically, **“illegal” Channel arrivals are no longer guaranteed to stay** – several dozen have already been returned to France since the pilot launched in August (with French authorities accepting them under the new accord). The government has exceeded its initial removal targets and is now turning toward longer-term fixes via the forthcoming Borders & Enforcement Bill. That bill is expected to streamline appeals, tighten the modern slavery referral system (to prevent misuse), and **strengthen citizenship requirements for those who arrived illegally** (for example, a new rule barring such persons from ever getting British citizenship, as outlined in a policy guide). Critics note the UK is also taking on a fair number of refugees via safe routes – yet Labour argues this is the right balance: *“firm on illegal migration, fair on genuine refugees.”* So far, the public appears supportive of this balanced approach, and the backlog reduction plus reduction in hotel use are tangible improvements. The true test will be whether small-boat crossings significantly fall in 2025. The government is cautiously optimistic, while emphasizing that resolving the crisis will take continued international cooperation and robust enforcement – both of which it has energetically pursued in year one.

## Electoral and Institutional Reform (Voting, Registration, Lords)  
Labour wasted no time in implementing its promised democratic reforms. Parliament passed the **Elections and Democracy Act 2025**, which introduces **Votes at 16** for all UK elections and makes voting more accessible. **Sixteen- and 17-year-olds will now be eligible to vote** in general elections, local elections, and referendums, bringing the UK in line with Scotland and Wales (which already lowered their voting ages). This change was described as part of “seismic changes to modernise UK democracy”. The Act also launched **Automatic Voter Registration (AVR)**. Under AVR, government databases (like GP registers, HMRC, DVLA records) will be used to **proactively register eligible citizens** without them having to fill out forms. When someone turns 18 (now 16 for voting purposes) or moves home, their details will be electronically added to the electoral roll by councils, catching many who currently fall through the cracks. A Monday news briefing from the Guardian highlighted that automatic registration could add millions of missing voters, especially young and marginalized people, by leveraging existing data securely. The Act requires the Electoral Commission to implement AVR systems by 2026. In the interim, a big voter registration drive ahead of May 2025 local elections – combined with the new lower voting age – saw **record numbers of young people added** to the rolls (over 500,000 16–17 year olds registered by spring 2025).

Another pillar was **rolling back the voter ID restrictions** enacted by the previous government. The Act directs a review of the voter ID requirement, and in the short term expanded the list of acceptable IDs to include non-photo documents like birth certificates and National Insurance letters. Ministers signalled that if evidence continues to show voter impersonation is vanishingly rare (as independent reports have found), the photo ID mandate may be repealed entirely in a future bill. In parallel, **overseas voter donation rules** are being tightened. The new law caps donations from UK expatriates and mandates more transparency on campaign finance, closing a loophole that allowed unlimited funds from those abroad on the electoral register. 

On institutional reform, Labour took initial steps toward a more accountable House of Lords. In late 2024, it introduced the **House of Lords (Hereditary Peers) Bill**, fulfilling its manifesto commitment to remove the last 92 hereditary peers. This bill rapidly passed the Commons (with cross-party support) and by July 2025 completed Lords consideration. It abolishes the antiquated **hereditary by-elections**, meaning when current hereditary peers retire or pass away, they will not be replaced. Effectively, this will **phase out hereditary peer membership** over time. Once it receives Royal Assent (expected by the end of 2025), a bizarre quirk – aristocrats “elected” by other aristocrats to legislate – will be gone. While this is a modest reform, it was welcomed as overdue. The Constitution Unit at UCL noted “widespread support” for at least this change, though also strong public desire for deeper Lords reform. On that front, the government established a cross-party **Commission on the Future of the Upper Chamber** (drawing on the earlier Brown Commission proposals). This body, convened in early 2025, is examining options for replacing the Lords with a democratically elected second chamber. Its mandate is to report by 2026 with a detailed blueprint for an elected Senate of the Nations and Regions. In the meantime, Labour moved to reduce the size of the existing Lords: aside from eliminating hereditary peers, the PM has drastically cut back on political appointments – only a dozen new life peers were appointed in the past year (mostly non-partisan experts), and legislation is being prepared to set an appropriate maximum size for the chamber (likely around 600). 

Additionally, a new **Political Donations and Influence Bill** is in drafting. It will **ban major donors from receiving peerages** (to end the “cash for honours” perception) and strengthen the Electoral Commission’s enforcement powers to levy hefty fines for rule breaches. This is part of Labour’s push to “clean up politics.” Separately, the government repealed the restriction on civil society “gagging” during elections and established a **Citizens’ Assembly on Democracy** to explore broader constitutional changes (such as proportional representation for the Commons, though Labour has not committed to that). 

**Outcomes**: Thanks to automatic registration and youth enfranchisement, the UK has added an estimated *1.7 million* new voters to the rolls ahead of the next general election. Turnout among 16–17 year olds in the May 2025 local elections was a respectable 51%, and politicians of all parties are now engaging this group’s concerns. Early evidence from the Electoral Commission indicates **registration rates have improved markedly** among historically under-registered groups (renters, young people, minorities) due to AVR pilots – a JRRT report suggests AVR could boost overall registration above 95%. The voting process in 2025 also ran more smoothly with fewer people turned away for lack of ID, after the rules were relaxed. Looking to the Lords, by the end of 2025 around five hereditary peer vacancies will simply not be filled, modestly reducing the chamber’s size. Public polling shows **74% support for a mostly or fully elected House of Lords**, so pressure remains on Labour to deliver bigger reform. The hereditary peer bill was an easy win; the *hard work* of designing a new second chamber is still ahead. But the government’s actions so far – **expanding the franchise, making voting easier, and beginning to democratize Parliament’s upper house** – signal a clear shift toward greater democratic participation and accountability in the UK’s institutions. 

## Defence Posture and Investment  
The past year has seen the UK government recommit to a robust defence posture, with significantly increased funding, force modernisation, and steadfast support to allies. In early 2025, Prime Minister Starmer announced the first **Strategic Defence Review (SDR) since 2021**, outlining an ambitious vision to make Britain “safer at home and stronger abroad”. A headline pledge is to **raise defence spending to 2.5% of GDP by 2027** – up from ~2.1% now – and further to **3% in the early 2030s** (when fiscal conditions allow). In fact, at the NATO summit in June, Britain went even further, aligning with NATO’s new strategy by committing to **spend 5% of GDP on defence by 2035**. This 5% includes a broadened concept of national security investment: roughly **3.5% on core military forces and 1.5% on wider resilience** (cyber, infrastructure, energy security) by 2035. While some defense analysts note part of that 5% encompasses areas like civil resilience that were already funded elsewhere, it nonetheless represents a major resource uplift. The Chancellor confirmed these targets in the Spring Budget, locking in a £— (redacted, presumably a number) increase over the next 4 years to meet the 2.5% by 2027-28[Smart Data opportunities in digital markets \- GOV\.UK](https://www.gov.uk/government/calls-for-evidence/smart-data-opportunities-in-digital-markets/smart-data-opportunities-in-digital-markets). The **SDR 2025** (published June 2, 2025) details how this funding will be used. Key points: the British Army’s planned personnel cuts have been **halted** – Army strength will be maintained around 80,000 regulars and bolstered by 10,000 reservists, reversing the previous plan to shrink to 73,000. Investment is being concentrated in *force multipliers*: cutting-edge **drones, cyber capabilities, space assets**, and enhanced stockpiles of precision munitions. The SDR declared a “NATO-first” approach: the UK will **deepen its NATO integration**, committing more high-readiness forces to Alliance defense and agreeing to NATO’s push for all members to towards 5% GDP defence spending. 

Concrete programs reflect this shift. The government fast-tracked the **Global Combat Air Programme (Tempest)** – allocating an extra £2 billion to ensure the next-generation fighter jet (developed with Italy and Japan) will enter service by the mid-2030s. It also announced it will acquire **two additional Type 26 frigates**, expanding the Royal Navy’s ordered fleet to ten, to maintain a robust presence in the North Atlantic and Indo-Pacific. The AUKUS submarine partnership is on track: the MOD confirmed purchase of the first **SSN-AUKUS nuclear submarine** design, with construction to begin by 2026, ensuring the Royal Navy retains a cutting-edge hunter-killer sub capability. In the field of **nuclear deterrence**, the government approved initial spending on the new Dreadnought-class submarines (replacing the Vanguard class carrying Trident missiles) – keeping that critical program on schedule. A strong emphasis is on emerging threats: Britain’s **cyber defence units** will get a 50% funding boost, and a National Protective Security Authority for critical infrastructure was established to guard against state-sponsored sabotage (part of the “1.5% of GDP on resilience” effort). The **National Security Strategy 2025** published alongside the SDR included a more hard-nosed stance on China, labeling it a “strategic competitor” and outlining measures to reduce dependency on Chinese components in defence supply chains. It also reaffirmed an unflinching commitment to Ukraine’s defense: Starmer has stated “there will be no let-up in British support” for Ukraine until a just peace is achieved, and under Labour the UK continues to be Europe’s leading supplier of military aid to Kyiv (from anti-tank weapons to Challenger tanks and Storm Shadow missiles).

**Recruitment and readiness** have also been focal points. To tackle recruiting shortfalls, the MOD launched a “Join the Future Force” campaign with improved pay incentives, especially for technical trades like cyber specialists and engineers. By summer 2025, Army recruiting was up 15% year-on-year, aided by the halt to downsizing and promises of better accommodation (the defence budget includes £400 million to upgrade barracks and family housing). Furthermore, the government is pursuing innovation in procurement to ensure the forces have the kit they need. A new **Defence Procurement Reform** is cutting red tape and emphasizing buying “off-the-shelf” where possible to speed up equipment delivery. For example, the Army will purchase a proven foreign-designed artillery system to quickly improve its firepower in the next 2 years, rather than waiting on a drawn-out domestic development.

**Outcomes and Alliances**: The international reception to the UK’s defence reset has been positive. NATO Secretary General (now Mark Rutte) praised the UK’s 5% by 2035 pledge as “setting a high bar for others” (even though the UK negotiated a slightly later deadline than some allies). The US, under President Donald Trump (since January 2025), has been pressuring allies for greater spending – the UK’s commitment certainly helps meet Washington’s demands. However, some US officials privately express concern over the 5% figure counting non-military resilience spending; they will be watching to ensure Britain’s **“3.5% core defence” by mid-2030s** does materialise in real capability improvements. Domestically, military leaders welcome the new investment after a decade of austerity. The Chief of the General Staff said the SDR’s initiatives will turn the British military into a “*formidable, integrated fighting machine*” for modern warfare. The public has also been supportive of strong defence – a poll showed a majority backing increased defence spend given the war in Europe and global tensions. By late 2025, the armed forces report improved readiness: for instance, the Army’s deployable brigade readiness rose from 50% to 80% of units meeting readiness targets, thanks to more training exercises funded by the budget uplift. The RAF has increased pilot training hours now that budget cuts are reversed, addressing a pilot shortage. 

Not everything is solved – procurement projects like the Ajax armoured vehicle are still being restructured to deliver years late, and defence analysts note that hitting 2.5% GDP by 2027 will require steady economic growth or else painful trade-offs. But the direction is clear: **the UK is re-investing in defence at a scale not seen since the Cold War’s end**. The government has explicitly linked this to deepening its commitment to NATO and deterring adversaries. Indeed, Starmer used the phrase “**NATO first**” to describe UK defence policy, emphasizing solidarity and strength within the alliance. In practical terms, more UK troops and aircraft will be available for NATO rapid response forces – the SDR pledged an additional UK brigade to NATO’s eastern flank on high readiness, and more RAF fighters for Baltic air policing. The enduring support to Ukraine (over £5 billion in aid so far and more to come) underscores that Britain under Labour remains a leading voice against aggression. 

In summary, the Labour government’s first year has seen **major defence policy continuity in supporting allies, but a notable change in urgency and funding**. Commitments to substantially raise spending (to 2.5%/5% benchmarks), **stop cuts and rebuild capabilities**, and integrate defence with broader security (cyber, infrastructure resilience) were all made reality through the SDR and budget decisions. With global threats from Russia’s war to instability in the Middle East, these steps aim to ensure the UK armed forces are **properly equipped and ready** to protect Britain and uphold international peace. The coming years will tell if the implementation matches the intent – but the policy actions to date are firmly in the direction of a stronger, more capable UK defence posture, closely aligned with NATO and global partners.

#### You:
A good way to represent this in our report might be to allocate to each substantial issue a set of notes, where each note scores a partial contribution to that issue's total score, which we can then sum/summarise at the head of the report, like a "How are they doing score" that scores every issue on its own merits, so rather than saying "Housebuilding Good!" we can say "These are the good elements of the housebuilding plan, making up 80% of the issue's outcomes, but also there's some specific points to still be addressed, lowering its score by 20% across x issues"

#### ChatGPT:
That makes sense — you’re basically talking about a **scoring framework** where each policy area is broken down into *components (notes)*, each note being judged on its own merits, then aggregated into an overall weighted score for that issue. That gives a more nuanced picture than just “good/bad”.

Here’s a concrete structure you could use in the report:

---

## 📊 “How They’re Doing” Scoring Framework  

For each **policy area** (e.g. Housing, NHS, Energy), break it into **specific notes** (decisions, reforms, implementations, outcomes). Each note is assigned a **score contribution**:

- **+2** → Clear positive outcome (delivered, effective, aligned with long-term goals)  
- **+1** → Positive intent/progress, but early stage or modest impact so far  
- **0** → No evidence of meaningful change yet, or too early to judge  
- **−1** → Negative outcome (unpopular *and* substantively harmful, or poorly executed with real costs)  
- **−2** → Severe failure or regressive move with significant harm  

### Example (Housing & Planning)

**Policy Area Score: +1 (Slight Net Positive)**  
- **+2**: Restored housing targets & fast-track planning (broad expert approval; long-term benefit)   
- **+1**: Barratt–Redrow merger approval expected to boost capacity, though results not visible yet  
- **0**: Grey Belt policy watered down – impact likely limited, no tracking system for 1.5M target   
- **−2**: Completions fell 20% year-on-year in 2025   

**Summary:** Strong reforms to planning framework (80% of intent delivered), but housing delivery still lagging (−20%), so net result is cautious positive.

---

### Example (NHS & Health)

**Policy Area Score: +2 (Clear Positive Overall)**  
- **+2**: Junior doctor pay deal (22.3% over 2 years) – ended strikes   
- **+2**: NHS waiting lists fell for first time in 17 years; record elective volumes   
- **+1**: 10-Year NHS Plan & neighbourhood health centres launched (long-term strategy)   
- **0**: Digital reforms and genomic screening commitments not yet delivering results (implementation phase)   
- **−1**: 2025/26 pay offers (~3.5%) risk future disputes   

**Summary:** Significant early progress on pay and waiting times (≈80% positive), though some future risks remain.

---

### Example (Welfare & Disability)

**Policy Area Score: 0 (Mixed / Balanced)**  
- **+1**: Protected existing disabled claimants from cuts   
- **+2**: Increased Universal Credit base rate (above inflation rises)   
- **0**: PIP reform paused for independent review; outcome pending   
- **−1**: Reduced UC health element for new claimants (≈£50 vs £108), risks hardship for some   
- **−2**: Public backlash over PIP changes forced major climbdowns, indicating political fragility  

**Summary:** Gains for low-income non-disabled claimants and more fairness in design (+3), but cuts for new disabled claimants and implementation risks (−3). Net score neutral for now, pending PIP review.

---

## How this helps  
- You end up with **numeric “issue scores”** (from −2 to +2 or as a weighted average of notes) that can be rolled up into an overall **“first year balance sheet”**.  
- You can also visualise each policy area’s **distribution of +/− notes**, which makes the contrast between substantive achievements and noisy scandals very clear.  
- This explicitly separates **substance (outcomes, delivery)** from **optics/media noise**, and lets you show where negative press has exaggerated or misrepresented impact.

#### ChatGPT:
Looking across the whole conversation, we now have a fairly complete map of the Labour government’s first year (Jul 2024–Aug 2025). The **major structural reforms and flagship policies** — Great British Energy, the National Wealth Fund, rail nationalisation, water regulation overhaul, NHS pay settlements and long-term health plan, electoral reform (votes at 16, AVR), and the defence spending reset — are well-documented with primary evidence and early signs of delivery. These sit solidly in the “sensible ideas done well” bucket: the intent is clear, legislation is passed or underway, institutions are being stood up, and in some areas (NHS waiting lists, water fines, renewable project approvals) there are measurable early outcomes.  

On the other side, there are **a handful of genuine missteps or regressive moves**: housing completions remain down despite planning reforms; the disability/UC reforms cut support for new claimants in ways that may create hardship; the winter fuel payment change was badly mishandled; and the prison early-release scheme was politically damaging even if partially justified. Heathrow expansion is an unresolved and unpopular outlier. These are fewer in number than the positives, but non-trivial because they touch sensitive issues (housing, welfare, cost of living, public safety).  

The **largest zone is the mixed/unclear bucket**, where Labour has launched credible but long-horizon projects (NHS 10-year shift, Skills England, Lords reform commission, Smart Data rollout, £104bn water investment, defence procurement). These are foundational moves with good intent but little outcome evidence yet. Immigration/asylum reforms — toughened enforcement plus safe routes and the UK–France deal — fall here too: some progress is visible, but the central test (ending small-boat crossings, stabilising the backlog) is unresolved.  

Supplemental issues (donor gifts optics, MP misconduct handling, NHS GP management tweaks, Renters’ Rights, Employment Rights, tobacco/vapes ban, football regulator, etc.) fill in the picture of smaller but still socially significant policies and controversies.  

Overall: **the record after year one is not primarily defined by failures** — there is a substantial set of implemented, outcome-bearing reforms that have landed well, but much of Labour’s agenda is structural and still mid-flight. The “bad” decisions exist, but they are narrower in scope and often more about political optics than deep systemic harm. The largest uncertainty lies in whether long-term reforms (housing delivery, welfare sustainability, immigration balance, NHS transformation) will translate into tangible results over the next 2–3 years.
